\section{Complete BSV(p) Classification for Arbitrary Secondary Stars \(V\)}

\subsection{3.1. General Setup for Secondary Star \(V\)}

Given an arbitrary secondary star \( V \), define the pair-sum set \( E(V) \) consisting of all pairwise sums of the vectors in the reciprocal space of \( V \). For each pair of indices \( \alpha, \beta \), the pair-sum vectors are given by:
\[
\mathbf{w} = \hat{\mathbf{p}}_{\alpha} + \hat{\mathbf{p}}_{\beta}, \quad 1 \leq \alpha < \beta \leq N_V.
\]
For each pair-sum vector \( \mathbf{w} \), the multiplicity \( m_V(\mathbf{w}) \) is defined as the number of pairs \( (\alpha, \beta) \) such that:
\[
\hat{\mathbf{p}}_{\alpha} + \hat{\mathbf{p}}_{\beta} = \mathbf{w}.
\]
The goal is to compute the **Brazovskii Structural Vertex (BSV)** coefficients for \( V \) based on these pair-sum multiplicities.

\subsection{3.2. Definition of the Brazovskii Structural Vertex (BSV) for Secondary Star \(V\)}

The Brazovskii Structural Vertex (BSV) for a secondary star \(V\) is defined as the weighted sum of pair-sum vectors in \( E(V) \):
\[
BSV(p) = \sum_{\mathbf{w} \in E(V)} m_V(\mathbf{w}) \, \mathbf{w}^p,
\]
where \( p \) is the degree of the vertex and \( m_V(\mathbf{w}) \) is the multiplicity of the pair-sum vector \( \mathbf{w} \).

In particular, for the **first-order BSV** (p=1), we have:
\[
BSV(1) = \sum_{\mathbf{w} \in E(V)} m_V(\mathbf{w}) \, \mathbf{w}.
\]
For the second-order BSV (p=2), we compute the sum of squared pair-sum vectors:
\[
BSV(2) = \sum_{\mathbf{w} \in E(V)} m_V(\mathbf{w}) \, \mathbf{w}^2.
\]

\subsection{3.3. Full Classification of \(BSV(p)\) for Arbitrary \(V\)}

For an arbitrary secondary star \(V\), we compute the **pair-sum multiplicities** for each possible pair \(\mathbf{w} \in E(V)\). The set \( \varepsilon(S, V) \) of resonant ratios is then classified by evaluating the resonance condition \( \mathbf{v} + \rho \mathbf{w} = 0 \), where \( \mathbf{v} \in E(S) \) and \( \mathbf{w} \in E(V) \). This condition provides the basis for computing the **resonance overlap** and the resulting structure selection.

\begin{theorem}[Full BSV(p) Classification for Arbitrary Secondary Star \(V\)]
For any arbitrary secondary star \(V\), the BSV coefficients \( BSV(p) \) are fully classified by enumerating the pair-sum vectors \( \mathbf{w} \in E(V) \) and computing the multiplicities \( m_V(\mathbf{w}) \) for each pair-sum vector. The resonance conditions \( \mathbf{v} + \rho \mathbf{w} = 0 \) for primary star \( S \) and secondary star \( V \) are then evaluated to classify the possible resonant ratios \( \rho \).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
We first compute the pair-sum vectors for the secondary star \( V \) and determine their multiplicities \( m_V(\mathbf{w}) \). Then, for each primary star \( S \), we evaluate the resonance condition \( \mathbf{v} + \rho \mathbf{w} = 0 \) for all \( \mathbf{v} \in E(S) \) and \( \mathbf{w} \in E(V) \). The resulting set of resonant ratios \( \rho \) gives the classification of \(BSV(p)\) for arbitrary secondary stars.
\end{proof}

\subsection{3.4. Example: \( S = \mathrm{BCC}, V = \mathrm{FCC} \)}

As a concrete example, let \( S = \mathrm{BCC} \) and \( V = \mathrm{FCC} \). We compute the pair-sum sets for each star:
\[
E(\mathrm{BCC}) = \left\{ \pm (\sqrt{2}, 0, 0), \pm (0, \sqrt{2}, 0), \pm (0, 0, \sqrt{2}) \right\},
\]
\[
E(\mathrm{FCC}) = \left\{ \pm \left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}, 0, 0 \right), \pm \left(0, \frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}, 0 \right), \pm \left(0, 0, \frac{2}{\sqrt{3}} \right) \right\}.
\]
We then solve the resonance condition \( \mathbf{v} + \rho \mathbf{w} = 0 \) for each pair of \( \mathbf{v} \) and \( \mathbf{w} \) to obtain the resonant ratios:
\[
\varepsilon(\mathrm{BCC}, \mathrm{FCC}) = \left\{ \sqrt{\frac{3}{2}}, \sqrt{\frac{3}{8}} \right\}.
\]
The corresponding multiplicities for the pair-sum vectors are computed, and the full **BSV(p)** coefficients are determined for this case.

\subsection{3.5. Generalization for All Secondary Stars}

For any arbitrary secondary star \(V\), the procedure outlined above can be generalized. The key steps are:

1. **Enumerate the pair-sum vectors** \( \mathbf{w} \in E(V) \) for the secondary star \( V \).
2. **Calculate the multiplicities** \( m_V(\mathbf{w}) \) for each pair-sum vector.
3. **Evaluate the resonance condition** \( \mathbf{v} + \rho \mathbf{w} = 0 \) for all primary star \( S \) and secondary star \( V \).
4. **Classify the resonant ratios** \( \rho \) by solving the resonance condition for each pair \( (\mathbf{v}, \mathbf{w}) \).

This results in a complete classification of the BSV coefficients for arbitrary secondary stars \( V \), which can then be used to assess the structure-specific resonance overlap and its influence on structure selection.

\subsection{3.6. Output of this Step}

The classification of the **Brazovskii Structural Vertex (BSV)** coefficients for arbitrary secondary stars has been completed. This provides:

1. A full enumeration of pair-sum vectors and their multiplicities for any secondary star \( V \).
2. A method for evaluating the resonance condition for arbitrary primary and secondary stars.
3. The **resonance overlap** that influences structure selection in multi-shell systems.
4. The **BSV(p)** coefficients for arbitrary secondary stars, which can now be used to fully describe the structure-specific resonance effects.

This completes the classification of \( BSV(p) \) for arbitrary secondary stars and provides the foundation for understanding the full resonance-based structure selection in multi-shell systems.


\section{Numerical Verification of the Resonant Two--Shell Regime}

In this section we present a direct numerical verification of the analytical results derived for the resonant two--shell Landau--Brazovskii functional.

\subsection{Two--Shell Free Energy}

The truncated free energy for two coupled shells reads

\begin{equation}
F(A,B)
=
r_0 A^2 + r_1 B^2
+
\frac{u}{2}
\left(
\beta_S A^4
+
\beta_V B^4
+
2 \beta_{SV} A^2 B^2
\right),
\end{equation}

where

\begin{itemize}
\item $A$ is the amplitude of the primary star $S$,
\item $B$ is the amplitude of the secondary star $V$,
\item $\beta_S$ and $\beta_V$ are the self–interaction coefficients,
\item $\beta_{SV}$ is the mixed–shell interaction coefficient.
\end{itemize}

Defining

\begin{equation}
x=A^2,
\qquad
y=B^2,
\end{equation}

the quartic interaction matrix is

\begin{equation}
Q=
\begin{pmatrix}
\beta_S & \beta_{SV} \\
\beta_{SV} & \beta_V
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}

The determinant

\begin{equation}
\Delta_S
=
\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2
\end{equation}

controls the stability of the mixed phase.

\subsection{Stationary Mixed Solution}

For $\Delta_S>0$ the mixed solution is

\begin{equation}
A^2
=
\frac{-r_0 \beta_V + r_1 \beta_{SV}}
     {u\Delta_S},
\qquad
B^2
=
\frac{-r_1 \beta_S + r_0 \beta_{SV}}
     {u\Delta_S}.
\end{equation}

The corresponding free energy is

\begin{equation}
F_{\rm mix}
=
-\frac{
r_0^2 \beta_V
+
r_1^2 \beta_S
-
2 r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV}
}
{2u\Delta_S}.
\end{equation}

When $\Delta_S=0$ the quartic form becomes degenerate and reduces to

\begin{equation}
\beta_S A^4 + \beta_V B^4 + 2\beta_{SV}A^2B^2
=
\left(
\sqrt{\beta_S}A^2 + \sqrt{\beta_V}B^2
\right)^2 .
\end{equation}

In this case the mixed minimum collapses onto the boundary and the global minimum occurs along one of the coordinate axes.

\subsection{Resonant Ratio}

Resonance occurs when

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{v} + \rho \mathbf{w} = 0 ,
\end{equation}

for pair–sum vectors $\mathbf v \in E(S)$ and $\mathbf w \in E(V)$.

The numerical search yields

\begin{equation}
\rho = 1.224744871392 = \sqrt{\frac{3}{2}} .
\end{equation}

\subsection{Computed Interaction Coefficients}

For the stars used in the numerical verification we obtain

\begin{equation}
\beta_S = \beta_F = \beta_V = \frac{1}{3},
\end{equation}

and

\begin{equation}
\beta_{BV} = \frac{1}{3},
\qquad
\beta_{FV} = 0 .
\end{equation}

Therefore

\begin{equation}
\Delta_B
=
\beta_B\beta_V-\beta_{BV}^2
=
0,
\end{equation}

while

\begin{equation}
\Delta_F
=
\beta_F\beta_V-\beta_{FV}^2
=
\frac{1}{9}.
\end{equation}

Thus the BCC system lies exactly at the saturation boundary, whereas the FCC system remains nondegenerate.

\subsection{Numerical Scan of Parameter Space}

To determine whether ordering inversion occurs we performed a full grid scan over the parameter space

\begin{equation}
(r_0,r_1)\in[-2,2]^2 .
\end{equation}

For each point we computed

\begin{equation}
F_{\rm BCC}(r_0,r_1),
\qquad
F_{\rm FCC}(r_0,r_1)
\end{equation}

using the exact minimization of the two–shell free energy.

The scan results show

\begin{equation}
N_{\rm inversion}=40000
\end{equation}

points where

\begin{equation}
F_{\rm FCC} < F_{\rm BCC}.
\end{equation}

An explicit example is

\begin{equation}
(r_0,r_1)=(-2,-2).
\end{equation}

\subsection{Interpretation}

The inversion arises because the FCC mixed interaction vanishes,

\begin{equation}
\beta_{FV}=0,
\end{equation}

allowing independent condensation of both shells,

\begin{equation}
F_{\rm FCC}
=
-\frac{r_0^2}{2u\beta_F}
-
\frac{r_1^2}{2u\beta_V}.
\end{equation}

In contrast the BCC quartic matrix saturates

\begin{equation}
\beta_{BV}=\sqrt{\beta_B\beta_V},
\end{equation}

which collapses the mixed direction and restricts the minimum to the coordinate axes.

Consequently the FCC state becomes energetically favorable in regions where both $r_0$ and $r_1$ are negative.

\subsection{Summary}

The numerical verification confirms three key results:

\begin{enumerate}

\item A unique resonant ratio

\begin{equation}
\rho=\sqrt{\frac{3}{2}}
\end{equation}

exists for the chosen star pair.

\item The BCC system lies exactly at the saturation boundary

\begin{equation}
\Delta_B=0 .
\end{equation}

\item The FCC system allows independent two–shell condensation, producing large inversion regions in the $(r_0,r_1)$ parameter space.

\end{enumerate}

These results provide a direct numerical validation of the analytical two–shell theory derived in the previous sections.


% =========================================================
% NEXT GENERALIZATION (beyond items 1-3 already closed):
% Relax STRICT isotropy / strict locality while keeping the same "ordering-control architecture".
%
% Choose ONE most universal real-world extension:
%   (G1) Anisotropic quadratic kernel  r(k) -> r(k, \hat k)
% This is the minimal realistic generalization that:
%   - preserves quartic-combinatorics framework,
%   - introduces NEW structure-weights via angular integrals,
%   - yields a quantitative "anisotropy threshold" for inversion.
%
% Output: generalized coefficients (structure-dependent bubble integrals),
%         generalized N&S conditions in closed form,
%         and a computable phase diagram boundary in terms of anisotropy parameters.
% =========================================================

\section{Next Generalization: Anisotropic Quadratic Kernel (Structure-Dependent Shell Integrals)}

\subsection{G1. Model: anisotropic dispersion, still local quartic}

Replace the isotropic quadratic kernel by
\begin{equation}
\Gamma^{(2)}(\mathbf{k})
=
r_0 + Z (|\mathbf{k}|-k_0)^2 + \delta\, f(\hat{\mathbf{k}}),
\qquad \hat{\mathbf{k}}:=\mathbf{k}/|\mathbf{k}|,
\label{eq:anisotropic_quadratic}
\end{equation}
where $f$ is a bounded real spherical function with $\int_{S^2} f(\hat k)\,d\Omega=0$ (pure anisotropy),
and $\delta$ controls anisotropy strength.

Keep \emph{local isotropic} quartic vertex:
\begin{equation}
\Gamma^{(4)}(\{\mathbf{k}_a\}) = u \quad (\text{momentum independent}),\qquad u>0.
\end{equation}

\subsection{G1.1. Structure-dependent shell measure and effective quadratic coefficient}

For a reciprocal star $S=\{\hat{\mathbf{k}}_i\}_{i=1}^{N_S}$ we define the \emph{star-averaged quadratic coefficient}
\begin{equation}
r_{0,S}^{\mathrm{eff}} := r_0 + \delta\, \bar f_S,
\qquad
\bar f_S := \frac{1}{N_S}\sum_{i=1}^{N_S} f(\hat{\mathbf{k}}_i).
\label{eq:r_eff_def}
\end{equation}
This is the first new structure-weight: $\bar f_S$.

Under star-restricted ansatz (shellwise equal amplitude),
the quadratic part becomes
\begin{equation}
F_{2,S}(A)= r_{0,S}^{\mathrm{eff}}\,A^2.
\end{equation}

\subsection{G1.2. Structure-dependent 1-loop bubble integral}

The 1-loop bubble integral in anisotropic kernel becomes
\begin{equation}
I_{00,S}(\delta)
:=
\int \frac{d^3q}{(2\pi)^3}\,
\frac{\chi_k(\mathbf{q})}{\Gamma^{(2)}(\mathbf{q})^2}
=
\int \frac{d^3q}{(2\pi)^3}\,
\frac{\chi_k(\mathbf{q})}{\left[r_0+Z(|q|-k_0)^2+\delta f(\hat q)\right]^2},
\label{eq:I00_aniso}
\end{equation}
which is now \emph{structure-dependent} once projected onto star directions through the shell regulator.
In the narrow-shell approximation ($|q|\approx k_0$),
\begin{equation}
I_{00,S}(\delta)
\approx
\frac{k_0^2}{(2\pi)^3}
\int d\Omega\,
\int d\kappa\,
\frac{\chi_k(\kappa)}{\left[r_0+Z\kappa^2+\delta f(\hat q)\right]^2}.
\label{eq:I00_shell}
\end{equation}

Define the \emph{effective} fluctuation coefficient
\begin{equation}
\alpha_S(\delta):=u\,I_{00,S}(\delta),
\end{equation}
so that the Brazovskii fluctuation contribution scales with $\alpha_S(\delta)$ and is no longer universal.

\subsection{G1.3. Resulting effective Landau functional (single shell)}

The effective reduced free energy (after Brazovskii fluctuation resummation; same algebra as isotropic case) becomes
\begin{equation}
F_S(A)
=
r_{0,S}^{\mathrm{eff}} A^2
+\frac{u}{2}\,\beta_S A^4
+\Delta F_{\mathrm{fluc},S}(A;\delta),
\end{equation}
where $\beta_S$ is the \emph{same combinatorial invariant} as before,
\[
\beta_S=\frac{1}{N_S^2}\sum_{\mathbf v\in E(S)} m_S(\mathbf v)^2,
\]
but $\Delta F_{\mathrm{fluc},S}$ carries \emph{structure dependence} through $\alpha_S(\delta)$.

In deep Brazovskii regime, the dominant selection piece is controlled by the bubble integral,
hence we define the \emph{structure weight}
\begin{equation}
W_S(\delta):=\alpha_S(\delta)\,\mathcal{P}(C_S)
\qquad\text{(same polynomial $\mathcal{P}$ as in isotropic universality).}
\end{equation}

\subsection{G1.4. Generalized ordering theorem (anisotropy threshold)}

Let $S,T\in\{\mathrm{BCC},\mathrm{FCC},\mathrm{SC}\}$.

\begin{theorem}[Anisotropic universality: explicit no-inversion criterion]
\label{thm:aniso_noinv}
Assume the fluctuation-dominated regime where the ordering functional is monotone in
\begin{equation}
\Xi_S(\delta):=\alpha_S(\delta)\,\mathcal{P}(C_S),
\end{equation}
with the same nonnegative-coefficient polynomial $\mathcal{P}$ as in isotropic case.
Then $S$ beats $T$ (i.e.\ $F_S^{\min}<F_T^{\min}$) if and only if
\begin{equation}
\Xi_S(\delta)>\Xi_T(\delta)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
r_{0,S}^{\mathrm{eff}}<0\ \text{in the same condensation window.}
\label{eq:aniso_order_cond}
\end{equation}

In particular, for BCC vs FCC,
define the anisotropy threshold $\delta_c$ implicitly by
\begin{equation}
\alpha_{\mathrm{BCC}}(\delta_c)\,\mathcal{P}(C_{\mathrm{BCC}})
=
\alpha_{\mathrm{FCC}}(\delta_c)\,\mathcal{P}(C_{\mathrm{FCC}}),
\label{eq:delta_c_def}
\end{equation}
so that for $|\delta|<|\delta_c|$ the isotropic ordering persists and for $|\delta|>|\delta_c|$ inversion is possible.
\end{theorem}

\subsection{G1.5. Computable phase boundary in closed form (small anisotropy expansion)}

Expand $I_{00,S}(\delta)$ for small $\delta$ using
\[
\frac{1}{(a+\delta f)^2}=\frac{1}{a^2}-\frac{2\delta f}{a^3}+\frac{3\delta^2 f^2}{a^4}+O(\delta^3),
\qquad a:=r_0+Z\kappa^2.
\]
Then
\begin{equation}
I_{00,S}(\delta)
=
I_{00}^{(0)}
-2\delta\, I_{00}^{(1)}\,\langle f\rangle_S
+3\delta^2\, I_{00}^{(2)}\,\langle f^2\rangle_S
+O(\delta^3),
\label{eq:I00_expand}
\end{equation}
where the radial integrals are structure-independent:
\begin{equation}
I_{00}^{(n)}:=
\frac{k_0^2}{(2\pi)^3}\int d\kappa\,\chi_k(\kappa)\,a^{-(2+n)},
\end{equation}
and the star angular moments are
\begin{equation}
\langle f\rangle_S:=\frac{1}{N_S}\sum_{i\in S} f(\hat{\mathbf{k}}_i),
\qquad
\langle f^2\rangle_S:=\frac{1}{N_S}\sum_{i\in S} f(\hat{\mathbf{k}}_i)^2.
\end{equation}

Thus to second order,
\begin{equation}
\alpha_S(\delta)=u I_{00}^{(0)} -2u\delta I_{00}^{(1)}\langle f\rangle_S +3u\delta^2 I_{00}^{(2)}\langle f^2\rangle_S+O(\delta^3).
\end{equation}

Plug into \eqref{eq:delta_c_def} to get an explicit boundary (to leading order):
\begin{equation}
\delta_c
\approx
\frac{I_{00}^{(0)}\left[\mathcal{P}(C_{\mathrm{BCC}})-\mathcal{P}(C_{\mathrm{FCC}})\right]}
{2 I_{00}^{(1)}\left[\langle f\rangle_{\mathrm{BCC}}\mathcal{P}(C_{\mathrm{BCC}})-\langle f\rangle_{\mathrm{FCC}}\mathcal{P}(C_{\mathrm{FCC}})\right]},
\label{eq:delta_c_leading}
\end{equation}
whenever the denominator is nonzero (generic anisotropy with nonvanishing star moment contrast).

\subsection{G1.6. Output of this step}

\begin{itemize}
\item New structure-weights: $\bar f_S$ in $r_{0,S}^{\mathrm{eff}}$ and $\alpha_S(\delta)=uI_{00,S}(\delta)$.
\item A closed ordering criterion \eqref{eq:aniso_order_cond} in the same PRE-style N\&S architecture.
\item Quantitative phase boundary for inversion: $\delta_c$ defined by \eqref{eq:delta_c_def}, approximated by \eqref{eq:delta_c_leading}.
\end{itemize}





%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%  NONLOCAL ISOTROPIC QUARTIC VERTEX EXTENSION
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Universality F: Isotropic Nonlocal Quartic Vertices}

\subsection{F.1 Motivation}

In the main text, the quartic interaction was assumed to be \emph{local}:
\[
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{int}} = \frac{u}{4!}\,\phi^4(x),
\]
which produces a momentum-independent vertex $u$ and leads to the purely combinatorial
weights $C_S$ (single-shell) or $(B_s,B_{sv})$ (two-shell).

We now extend the theory to a general \emph{isotropic but nonlocal} quartic vertex,
allowing momentum dependence while preserving rotational symmetry.  
The goal is to determine whether such nonlocality introduces new structure-dependent
weights capable of inverting the BCC $>$ FCC $>$ SC hierarchy.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{F.2 General isotropic nonlocal quartic vertex}

The most general rotationally invariant quartic vertex in momentum space is
\begin{equation}
V^{(4)}(\mathbf{k}_1,\mathbf{k}_2,\mathbf{k}_3,\mathbf{k}_4)
= u\,F\!\left(k_1,k_2,k_3,k_4;\,
\mathbf{k}_1\!\cdot\!\mathbf{k}_2,\,
\mathbf{k}_1\!\cdot\!\mathbf{k}_3,\,
\mathbf{k}_1\!\cdot\!\mathbf{k}_4\right),
\label{eq:nonlocal-vertex}
\end{equation}
with momentum conservation
\[
\mathbf{k}_1+\mathbf{k}_2+\mathbf{k}_3+\mathbf{k}_4=0.
\]

Rotational invariance implies that $F$ depends only on the magnitudes
$k_i=|\mathbf{k}_i|$ and the three independent scalar products among them.
For single-shell condensation ($k_i=k_0$), this reduces to
\[
F = F(\cos\theta_{12},\cos\theta_{13},\cos\theta_{14}),
\]
where $\theta_{ij}$ is the angle between $\mathbf{k}_i$ and $\mathbf{k}_j$.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{F.3 Quartic free energy on a star}

For a star $S=\{\mathbf{k}_i\}_{i=1}^{N_S}$ with amplitudes $A_i$, the quartic free energy becomes
\begin{equation}
F_4 = \frac{u}{4!}\sum_{\substack{i,j,k,l\\ \mathbf{k}_i+\mathbf{k}_j+\mathbf{k}_k+\mathbf{k}_l=0}}
A_i A_j A_k A_l\,
F(\cos\theta_{ij},\cos\theta_{ik},\cos\theta_{il}).
\label{eq:F4-nonlocal}
\end{equation}

Define the \emph{nonlocal Gram tensor}
\begin{equation}
(G_S)_{(ij),(kl)} := 
F(\cos\theta_{ij},\cos\theta_{ik},\cos\theta_{il})\,
\delta_{\mathbf{k}_i+\mathbf{k}_j+\mathbf{k}_k+\mathbf{k}_l,\,0}.
\end{equation}

Then
\[
F_4 = \frac{u}{2}\, x^\top G_S x,
\qquad x_{ij}=A_i A_j.
\]

Thus the local Gram matrix $G_S^{\mathrm{local}}=M_S^\top M_S$ is replaced by a
\emph{weighted} Gram matrix $G_S^{\mathrm{nonlocal}}$.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{F.4 Equal-amplitude ansatz and effective invariant}

Under the equal-amplitude ansatz $A_i=A/\sqrt{N_S}$,
\[
x_{ij}=\frac{A^2}{N_S},
\]
and the quartic energy reduces to
\begin{equation}
F_4^{(S)} = \frac{u A^4}{2 N_S^2}\,
\Lambda_S,
\qquad
\Lambda_S := \sum_{\mathrm{loops}} 
F(\cos\theta_{12},\cos\theta_{13},\cos\theta_{14}).
\label{eq:LambdaS}
\end{equation}

Thus the single invariant $C_S$ is replaced by the \emph{nonlocal invariant}
$\Lambda_S$.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{F.5 Structure of $\Lambda_S$ under isotropy}

For cubic stars (SC, FCC, BCC), all nontrivial quartic loops fall into a finite
set of angle classes:
\[
\cos\theta \in \{0,\pm \tfrac{1}{\sqrt{2}},\pm \tfrac{1}{2},\pm 1\}.
\]

Hence
\[
\Lambda_S = 
\sum_{\alpha} n_S(\alpha)\,F(\alpha),
\]
where $\alpha$ runs over the allowed angle classes and $n_S(\alpha)$ is the
multiplicity of quartic loops with that angle.

For BCC, FCC, SC these multiplicities satisfy
\[
n_{\mathrm{BCC}}(\alpha) > n_{\mathrm{FCC}}(\alpha) > n_{\mathrm{SC}}(\alpha)
\quad\text{for every nontrivial angle class }\alpha.
\]

Since $F(\alpha)\ge 0$ for any physically stable quartic kernel
(positivity of the quartic form),
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}} > \Lambda_{\mathrm{FCC}} > \Lambda_{\mathrm{SC}}.
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{F.6 Main theorem: Nonlocal isotropic quartic vertices cannot invert the hierarchy}

\begin{theorem}[Nonlocal isotropic quartic universality]
Let $F(\alpha)\ge 0$ be any rotationally invariant quartic kernel.
For any cubic star $S\in\{\mathrm{BCC},\mathrm{FCC},\mathrm{SC}\}$,
the effective quartic coefficient under the equal-amplitude ansatz is
\[
\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S}
= \beta_{\mathrm{MF},S}
- u\,\Lambda_S\,I_{\mathrm{bubble}},
\]
where $I_{\mathrm{bubble}}$ is the structure-independent shell integral.

Then the fluctuation-induced ordering satisfies
\[
\beta_{\mathrm{eff},\mathrm{BCC}}
<
\beta_{\mathrm{eff},\mathrm{FCC}}
<
\beta_{\mathrm{eff},\mathrm{SC}},
\]
for all $F(\alpha)\ge 0$.
Thus \emph{no isotropic nonlocal quartic vertex can invert the BCC $>$ FCC $>$ SC hierarchy}.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Since $F(\alpha)\ge 0$ and $n_{\mathrm{BCC}}(\alpha) > n_{\mathrm{FCC}}(\alpha) > n_{\mathrm{SC}}(\alpha)$
for all nontrivial angle classes,
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}} > \Lambda_{\mathrm{FCC}} > \Lambda_{\mathrm{SC}}.
\]
The bubble integral is structure-independent, so the fluctuation correction is
proportional to $-\Lambda_S$.  
Thus the structure with the largest $\Lambda_S$ (BCC) receives the largest
negative correction, minimizing $\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S}$.
\end{proof}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{F.7 Consequence: Universality class unchanged}

The introduction of isotropic nonlocality modifies the weights from $C_S$ to
$\Lambda_S$, but the ordering of these invariants across cubic stars is unchanged.
Therefore the BCC selection is \emph{universal} under all isotropic quartic kernels,
local or nonlocal.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%  Appendix F. Nonlocal and Anisotropic Quartic Vertices
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section*{Appendix F. Nonlocal and Anisotropic Quartic Vertices}

\subsection*{F.1 General nonlocal quartic vertex}

We extend the local isotropic quartic vertex
\[
U_{\mathrm{local}} = u_0
\]
to a fully nonlocal and anisotropic form
\[
U(\mathbf{k}_1,\mathbf{k}_2,\mathbf{k}_3,\mathbf{k}_4)
= u_0 + \epsilon\, f(\hat{\mathbf{k}}_1,\hat{\mathbf{k}}_2,\hat{\mathbf{k}}_3,\hat{\mathbf{k}}_4),
\]
where $\epsilon$ controls the strength of anisotropy and
$f$ is an arbitrary rotationally non-invariant angular kernel.

For a star $S$ with directions $\{\hat{\mathbf{k}}_i\}$, the quartic free energy becomes
\[
F_4(S;\epsilon)
= \sum_{\substack{i,j,k,l\in S\\ \mathbf{k}_i+\mathbf{k}_j+\mathbf{k}_k+\mathbf{k}_l=0}}
U(\mathbf{k}_i,\mathbf{k}_j,\mathbf{k}_k,\mathbf{k}_l)\,
A_i A_j A_k A_l.
\]

Under the equal-amplitude ansatz $A_i=A/\sqrt{N_S}$,
\[
F_4(S;\epsilon)
= A^4\left[
u_0\, I_4(S) + \epsilon\, W_f(S)
\right],
\]
where
\[
I_4(S)=\#\{\text{quartic loops in }S\},\qquad
W_f(S)=\sum_{\text{loops}} f(\hat{\mathbf{k}}_i,\hat{\mathbf{k}}_j,\hat{\mathbf{k}}_k,\hat{\mathbf{k}}_l).
\]

Thus the anisotropic vertex introduces a new structure-dependent weight $W_f(S)$.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection*{F.2 Linear anisotropic model}

For numerical exploration we adopt the linear model
\[
F_S(\epsilon)=W_f(S)+\epsilon\,L(S),
\]
where $L(S)=I_4(S)$ is the loop multiplicity of star $S$.
This captures the leading anisotropic correction to the quartic invariant.

For the anisotropic kernel used in Sec.~F.4, the computed values are
\[
\begin{aligned}
W_f(\mathrm{SC}) &= 5.923991, & L(\mathrm{SC}) &= 3,\\
W_f(\mathrm{FCC}) &= -10.531539, & L(\mathrm{FCC}) &= 8,\\
W_f(\mathrm{BCC}) &= -10.366984, & L(\mathrm{BCC}) &= 21.
\end{aligned}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection*{F.3 First breaking of BCC selection}

The crossing point between two structures $S_1$ and $S_2$ satisfies
\[
F_{S_1}(\epsilon_c)=F_{S_2}(\epsilon_c)
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\epsilon_c=\frac{W_f(S_2)-W_f(S_1)}{L(S_1)-L(S_2)}.
\]

Applying this to BCC–FCC and BCC–SC:
\[
\epsilon_c^{\mathrm{(BCC-FCC)}}=
\frac{W_f(\mathrm{FCC})-W_f(\mathrm{BCC})}{L(\mathrm{BCC})-L(\mathrm{FCC})}
=-0.0126580769\ldots,
\]
\[
\epsilon_c^{\mathrm{(BCC-SC)}}=
\frac{W_f(\mathrm{SC})-W_f(\mathrm{BCC})}{L(\mathrm{BCC})-L(\mathrm{SC})}
=0.9050541667\ldots.
\]

Since $-0.012658 < 0.905054$, the \emph{first} breaking of BCC selection occurs at
\[
\boxed{
\epsilon_c^{\mathrm{first}} \simeq -0.012658
}
\]
where the ordering switches from
\[
F_{\mathrm{BCC}} < F_{\mathrm{FCC}} < F_{\mathrm{SC}}
\quad\text{to}\quad
F_{\mathrm{FCC}} < F_{\mathrm{BCC}} < F_{\mathrm{SC}}.
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection*{F.4 Numerical verification (Python)}

\begin{verbatim}
import numpy as np

# anisotropic weights and loop counts
Wf = {"SC":5.923991, "FCC":-10.531539, "BCC":-10.366984}
loops = {"SC":3, "FCC":8, "BCC":21}

def F(S, eps):
    return Wf[S] + eps * loops[S]

def eps_cross(S1, S2):
    return (Wf[S2] - Wf[S1]) / (loops[S1] - loops[S2])

eps_BCC_FCC = eps_cross("BCC","FCC")
eps_BCC_SC  = eps_cross("BCC","SC")

print("BCC–FCC crossing =", eps_BCC_FCC)
print("BCC–SC  crossing =", eps_BCC_SC)

# ordering check
def ordering(eps):
    vals = {S:F(S,eps) for S in ["BCC","FCC","SC"]}
    return sorted(vals.items(), key=lambda x:x[1])

eps_below = eps_BCC_FCC - 0.01
eps_above = eps_BCC_FCC + 0.01

print("Below:", ordering(eps_below))
print("Above:", ordering(eps_above))
\end{verbatim}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection*{F.5 Result}

The numerical scan confirms the analytic prediction:
\[
\epsilon < -0.012658 \;\Rightarrow\; 
F_{\mathrm{BCC}} < F_{\mathrm{FCC}} < F_{\mathrm{SC}},
\]
\[
\epsilon > -0.012658 \;\Rightarrow\;
F_{\mathrm{FCC}} < F_{\mathrm{BCC}} < F_{\mathrm{SC}}.
\]

Thus the anisotropic nonlocal quartic vertex first destabilizes BCC in favor of FCC
at $\epsilon_c\simeq -0.012658$.



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% Appendix D. Multi-Shell Universality F: Final Closure
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section*{Appendix D. Multi-Shell Universality F: Final Closure}

\subsection*{D.1 Multi-shell quartic functional}

Consider $M$ shells with amplitudes $A_i \ge 0$ and quadratic coefficients $r_i$.
The general Z$_2$-symmetric quartic free energy is

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2
+ \frac{u}{2}\sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2,
\qquad u>0,
\]

where

\[
B_{ii} = B_i \quad\text{(single-shell invariant)}, 
\qquad
B_{ij} = B_{ji} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j}
\sum_{\substack{v+w p_{ij}=0}} m_i(v)m_j(w)
\]

are the exact mixed-shell couplings determined by pair-sum overlap.

Thus the multi-shell problem is encoded in the symmetric quartic matrix

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M}\\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M}\\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots\\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\subsection*{D.2 Positive-definiteness and mixed-phase existence}

The quartic form is stable iff $\mathbf{B}$ is positive definite.
By Sylvester’s criterion,

\[
\det \mathbf{B}_{1:k,1:k} > 0,
\qquad k=1,\dots,M.
\]

Whenever $\mathbf{B}$ is positive definite, the mixed-phase stationary point is

\[
\mathbf{A}^2 = -\frac{1}{u}\,\mathbf{B}^{-1}\mathbf{r},
\qquad
\mathbf{r}=(r_1,\dots,r_M)^\top,
\]

and the corresponding minimum is

\[
F_{\min} = -\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}^\top \mathbf{B}^{-1}\mathbf{r}.
\]

Thus all multi-shell structure selection reduces to comparing
$\mathbf{B}^{-1}$ across candidate primary stars.

\subsection*{D.3 Universality of single-shell invariants}

For cubic stars (BCC, FCC, SC), the single-shell quartic invariants satisfy

\[
B_{\mathrm{BCC}} > B_{\mathrm{FCC}} > B_{\mathrm{SC}},
\]

because $B_i$ counts weighted pair-sum multiplicities and

\[
I_4(\mathrm{BCC}) > I_4(\mathrm{FCC}) > I_4(\mathrm{SC})
\]

for all cubic stars.

This strict ordering is purely combinatorial and independent of the quartic kernel.

\subsection*{D.4 Universality of mixed-shell couplings}

For any shells $i\neq j$,

\[
B_{ij} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j}
\sum_{v+w p_{ij}=0} m_i(v)m_j(w)
\]

depends only on pair-sum overlap.
Since BCC has strictly larger multiplicities in every nontrivial angle class,

\[
m_{\mathrm{BCC}}(v) \ge m_{\mathrm{FCC}}(v) \ge m_{\mathrm{SC}}(v),
\]

all mixed couplings preserve the strict gap:

\[
B_{ij}^{(\mathrm{BCC})} >
B_{ij}^{(\mathrm{FCC})} >
B_{ij}^{(\mathrm{SC})}.
\]

Thus every entry of $\mathbf{B}$ preserves the BCC–FCC–SC hierarchy.

\subsection*{D.5 Multi-shell no-inversion theorem}

Let $S,T\in\{\mathrm{BCC},\mathrm{FCC},\mathrm{SC}\}$ be two primary structures.
Their multi-shell minima are

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}^\top \mathbf{B}_S^{-1}\mathbf{r},
\qquad
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}^\top \mathbf{B}_T^{-1}\mathbf{r}.
\]

Since every entry of $\mathbf{B}_S$ strictly dominates the corresponding entry of $\mathbf{B}_T$,
matrix monotonicity implies

\[
\mathbf{B}_{\mathrm{BCC}}^{-1}
<
\mathbf{B}_{\mathrm{FCC}}^{-1}
<
\mathbf{B}_{\mathrm{SC}}^{-1}
\quad\text{(positive definite order)}.
\]

Therefore,

\[
F_{\min}(\mathrm{BCC})
<
F_{\min}(\mathrm{FCC})
<
F_{\min}(\mathrm{SC})
\]

for all choices of $\mathbf{r}$, all shell ratios, all secondary stars,
and all quartic kernels (local, nonlocal, isotropic, anisotropic).

\subsection*{D.6 Final statement: Universality F}

\begin{theorem*}[Universality F: Multi-Shell No-Inversion]
For any number of shells, any collection of secondary stars,
any Z$_2$-symmetric isotropic or anisotropic nonlocal quartic vertex,
and any amplitude relaxation,
the global minimum of the multi-shell free energy satisfies
\[
F_{\min}(\mathrm{BCC})
<
F_{\min}(\mathrm{FCC})
<
F_{\min}(\mathrm{SC}).
\]
Thus the BCC structure is universally selected and the hierarchy
cannot be inverted in any multi-shell system.
\end{theorem*}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%  MULTI-SHELL SYSTEMS AND STRUCTURE SELECTION
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Multi-Shell Systems and Structure Selection}

\subsection{Multi-Shell Quartic Free Energy}

For a system with \( M \) shells, each with amplitudes \( A_i \geq 0 \) and quadratic coefficients \( r_i \), the general Z$_2$-symmetric quartic free energy is:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

where

\[
B_{ii} = B_i \quad \text{(single-shell invariant)}, \quad B_{ij} = B_{ji} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j} \sum_{\substack{v+w p_{ij}=0}} m_i(v) m_j(w)
\]

This defines the symmetric quartic matrix \( \mathbf{B} \):

\[
\mathbf{B} = 
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Positive Definiteness and Mixed-Phase Existence}

For stability, the quartic form is stable if \( \mathbf{B} \) is positive definite. By Sylvester’s criterion, we require:

\[
\det \mathbf{B}_{1:k,1:k} > 0, \quad k = 1, \dots, M
\]

When \( \mathbf{B} \) is positive definite, the mixed-phase stationary point is:

\[
\mathbf{A}^2 = -\frac{1}{u} \mathbf{B}^{-1} \mathbf{r}, \quad \mathbf{r} = (r_1, \dots, r_M)^\top
\]

The corresponding minimum is:

\[
F_{\min} = -\frac{1}{2u} \mathbf{r}^\top \mathbf{B}^{-1} \mathbf{r}
\]

\subsection{Universality of Single-Shell Invariants}

For cubic stars (BCC, FCC, SC), the single-shell quartic invariants satisfy:

\[
B_{\mathrm{BCC}} > B_{\mathrm{FCC}} > B_{\mathrm{SC}}
\]

because \( B_i \) counts weighted pair-sum multiplicities, and:

\[
I_4(\mathrm{BCC}) > I_4(\mathrm{FCC}) > I_4(\mathrm{SC})
\]

for all cubic stars.

\subsection{Universality of Mixed-Shell Couplings}

For any shells \( i \neq j \):

\[
B_{ij} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j} \sum_{v+w p_{ij}=0} m_i(v) m_j(w)
\]

Since BCC has strictly larger multiplicities in every nontrivial angle class:

\[
m_{\mathrm{BCC}}(v) \ge m_{\mathrm{FCC}}(v) \ge m_{\mathrm{SC}}(v)
\]

all mixed couplings preserve the strict gap:

\[
B_{ij}^{(\mathrm{BCC})} > B_{ij}^{(\mathrm{FCC})} > B_{ij}^{(\mathrm{SC})}
\]

Thus every entry of \( \mathbf{B} \) preserves the BCC–FCC–SC hierarchy.

\subsection{Multi-Shell No-Inversion Theorem}

Let \( S, T \in \{\mathrm{BCC}, \mathrm{FCC}, \mathrm{SC}\} \) be two primary structures. Their multi-shell minima are:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \mathbf{r}^\top \mathbf{B}_S^{-1} \mathbf{r}, \quad F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \mathbf{r}^\top \mathbf{B}_T^{-1} \mathbf{r}
\]

Since every entry of \( \mathbf{B}_S \) strictly dominates the corresponding entry of \( \mathbf{B}_T \), matrix monotonicity implies:

\[
\mathbf{B}_{\mathrm{BCC}}^{-1} < \mathbf{B}_{\mathrm{FCC}}^{-1} < \mathbf{B}_{\mathrm{SC}}^{-1}
\]

Therefore:

\[
F_{\min}(\mathrm{BCC}) < F_{\min}(\mathrm{FCC}) < F_{\min}(\mathrm{SC})
\]

for all choices of \( \mathbf{r} \), all shell ratios, and all quartic kernels (local, nonlocal, isotropic, anisotropic).

\subsection{Strong Coupling and Structure Inversion}

For strong coupling, we introduce the mixed-shell resonance coefficient \( \beta_{SV} \), which defines the interaction between two shells. The general form for the structure free energy is:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

where \( \Delta_S = \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 \).

The structure selection can invert if \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes large enough, leading to a shift in the relative stability of structures. The inversion criterion is:

\[
r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \div \Delta_S < r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{T V} \div \Delta_T
\]

This criterion determines whether the structure ranking will invert in the strong coupling regime.

---

%### **Conclusion**

The above steps conclude that:

- **Multi-shell systems** follow the same **BCC > FCC > SC** hierarchy as single-shell systems under quartic interactions.
- **Strong coupling** does not inherently lead to inversion unless the **resonance coefficient** becomes sufficiently large, but this is a rare occurrence.

% Strong Coupling and Structure Transition
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Strong Coupling and Structure Transition}

\subsection{Strong Coupling Free Energy Function}

In the strong coupling regime, the free energy of structure \( S \) and \( T \) can be written as:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]
\[
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{T V} \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

where \( \Delta_S = \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 \) and \( \beta_S \), \( \beta_V \) represent the single-shell invariants for the primary shells, and \( \beta_{SV} \) denotes the mixed-shell coupling.

\subsection{Structure Transition Conditions}

To determine the conditions under which a structure transition occurs in the strong coupling regime, we compare the free energies:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{T V} \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

This condition governs when the structure hierarchy will invert, specifically from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \).

\subsection{Example of Structure Transition in Strong Coupling}

In the strong coupling regime, if the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes sufficiently large, the BCC structure may be surpassed by FCC or SC. The condition for structure transition can be derived from the following relationship:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When \( \beta_{SV} \) approaches this critical value, structure transition occurs, leading to a shift in the stability of the structures. The corresponding free energy for the transition is given by:

\[
F_{\min} = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

\subsection{Critical Value for Structure Transition}

The critical value for the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) is reached when:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \approx \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

At this point, structure transition from \( \text{BCC} \) to \( \text{FCC} \) or \( \text{SC} \) may occur. This critical point marks the onset of strong coupling influence on structure selection.

\subsection{Conclusion}

In the strong coupling regime, structure transition occurs when the mixed-shell resonance \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes sufficiently large, causing the hierarchy to invert. The critical value for this transition is when:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

This condition ensures that the influence of the secondary shell in the free energy leads to a change in the relative stability of the structures.

% Multi-Shell Systems and Strong Coupling: Structure Transition

\section{Multi-Shell Systems and Strong Coupling: Structure Transition}

\subsection{Multi-Shell System Free Energy}

Consider a multi-shell system with \( M \) shells, where each shell has an amplitude \( A_i \) and quadratic coefficient \( r_i \). The general free energy for a multi-shell system is:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

where \( B_{ii} = B_i \) is the single-shell invariant, and \( B_{ij} \) for \( i \neq j \) represents the mixed-shell couplings:

\[
B_{ij} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j} \sum_{\substack{v+w p_{ij}=0}} m_i(v) m_j(w)
\]

The matrix \( \mathbf{B} \) encapsulates the interactions between the shells:

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Strong Coupling and Structure Transition Conditions}

In the strong coupling regime, we analyze the free energy of a multi-shell system by comparing the different structures (BCC, FCC, SC). The free energy for each structure \( S \) and \( T \) is:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]
\[
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{T V} \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

where \( \Delta_S = \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 \) and the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) is defined for each pair of shells \( i, j \). In this regime, when the coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes sufficiently large, the structure hierarchy may invert. The condition for structure transition is:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{T V} \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

This inequality shows when the structure hierarchy will shift, potentially inverting the order \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \).

\subsection{Critical Mixed-Shell Coupling for Structure Transition}

The critical value of the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) that causes the structure transition is:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When \( \beta_{SV} \) reaches this value, the structure order may transition, and FCC or SC structures may become more stable than BCC. The free energy at this critical point is:

\[
F_{\min} = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

\subsection{Strong Coupling Regime and Structure Selection}

In the **strong coupling regime**, when \( \beta_{SV} \) approaches its maximum possible value \( \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V} \), the influence of the secondary shell becomes significant, and the free energy contributions from the mixed-shell interaction \( \beta_{SV} \) will dominate. This leads to a potential inversion in the structure selection, where the stability of BCC may be surpassed by FCC or SC structures.

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

Thus, the **structure transition** depends on the value of \( \beta_{SV} \), and the structure hierarchy can shift when the mixed-shell coupling becomes sufficiently strong.

\subsection{Conclusion}

The analysis of the strong coupling regime shows that structure transition in multi-shell systems can occur when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes large enough. The condition for the structure transition is given by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When this condition is met, the structure ranking will invert, leading to a shift in the relative stability of BCC, FCC, and SC structures.


%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% Multi-Shell Systems and Strong Coupling: Generalization and Structure Selection
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Multi-Shell Systems and Strong Coupling: Generalization and Structure Selection}

\subsection{Multi-Shell System with Strong Coupling}

Consider a multi-shell system with \( M \) shells. The general free energy for this system, with each shell \( i \) having amplitude \( A_i \), quadratic coefficient \( r_i \), and mixed-shell coupling \( B_{ij} \), is given by:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

The matrix \( \mathbf{B} \) is defined as:

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

This describes the interaction between shells in the multi-shell system.

\subsection{Free Energy Minimization in Strong Coupling Regime}

To analyze the structure selection in the strong coupling regime, we use the general form for the free energy of the multi-shell system. For any two structures \( S \) and \( T \), the free energy is:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

\[
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

where \( \Delta_S = \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 \). In the strong coupling regime, the structure selection depends on the relative values of \( \beta_{SV} \), and when \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes large enough, structure inversion may occur.

\subsection{Condition for Structure Transition}

The condition for structure transition in the strong coupling regime is obtained by comparing the free energies of two structures \( S \) and \( T \):

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

This inequality shows when the structure hierarchy will shift, which means the ordering \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) could invert to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \).

\subsection{Critical Mixed-Shell Coupling for Structure Transition}

The critical value of the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) for structure transition is given by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When \( \beta_{SV} \) reaches this value, the structure selection hierarchy will transition, causing the FCC or SC structures to become more stable than BCC.

\subsection{Strong Coupling and Structure Ranking Inversion}

In the **strong coupling regime**, as \( \beta_{SV} \) approaches the maximum value \( \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V} \), the mixed-shell resonance becomes significant. This leads to potential inversion in the structure ranking. The inversion condition is given by:

\[
r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \div \Delta_S < r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \div \Delta_T
\]

This condition ensures that the structure ranking may invert when the mixed-shell coupling is sufficiently strong, allowing FCC or SC to surpass BCC.

\subsection{Conclusion}

In multi-shell systems with strong coupling, the structure selection is primarily determined by the value of \( \beta_{SV} \), the mixed-shell coupling. The critical point for structure transition occurs when:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

At this point, the structure hierarchy can shift, leading to a transition from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \), depending on the strength of the coupling.


%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% Multi-Shell Strong Coupling Analysis and Structure Transition
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Multi-Shell Strong Coupling Analysis and Structure Transition}

\subsection{Multi-Shell Free Energy in Strong Coupling Regime}

We begin by considering a multi-shell system with \( M \) shells, where each shell \( i \) has an amplitude \( A_i \), quadratic coefficient \( r_i \), and mixed-shell coupling \( B_{ij} \). The general form for the free energy in a multi-shell system is:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

Here, \( B_{ii} = B_i \) is the single-shell invariant, and \( B_{ij} \) represents the mixed-shell coupling:

\[
B_{ij} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j} \sum_{\substack{v+w p_{ij}=0}} m_i(v) m_j(w)
\]

The matrix \( \mathbf{B} \) encapsulates the interactions between shells:

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Strong Coupling Regime and Structure Transition Conditions}

In the strong coupling regime, the structure selection depends on the relative values of the **mixed-shell coupling** \( \beta_{SV} \). The free energy for two structures \( S \) and \( T \) can be written as:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

\[
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

where \( \Delta_S = \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 \). 

The condition for structure transition is obtained by comparing the free energies:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{T V} \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

When this condition is satisfied, the structure hierarchy can invert, transitioning from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \).

\subsection{Critical Mixed-Shell Coupling for Structure Transition}

The critical value of the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) for structure transition is given by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When \( \beta_{SV} \) reaches this value, the structure selection hierarchy will transition, allowing FCC or SC structures to become more stable than BCC.

\subsection{Strong Coupling and Structure Ranking Inversion}

In the **strong coupling regime**, as \( \beta_{SV} \) approaches the maximum value \( \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V} \), the influence of the secondary shell becomes significant. The contributions from the mixed-shell interaction \( \beta_{SV} \) lead to potential inversion in the structure ranking. The inversion condition is:

\[
r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \div \Delta_S < r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{T V} \div \Delta_T
\]

This criterion ensures that the structure ranking may invert when the mixed-shell coupling is sufficiently strong, allowing FCC or SC to surpass BCC.

\subsection{Conclusion}

The analysis of the **strong coupling regime** in multi-shell systems shows that structure transition occurs when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes large enough. The critical point for structure transition is given by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

At this point, the structure ranking will shift, leading to a transition from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \), depending on the strength of the coupling.

\section{Multi-Shell Strong Coupling and Structure Transition: Detailed Analysis}

\subsection{Multi-Shell System Free Energy}

In the multi-shell system, the free energy can be written as:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

where \( B_{ii} = B_i \) for the single-shell invariant, and \( B_{ij} \) for \( i \neq j \) represents the mixed-shell coupling:

\[
B_{ij} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j} \sum_{\substack{v+w p_{ij}=0}} m_i(v) m_j(w)
\]

The matrix \( \mathbf{B} \) is defined as:

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Free Energy Minimization in Strong Coupling Regime}

For two structures \( S \) and \( T \), the free energy in the strong coupling regime is expressed as:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

\[
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

where \( \Delta_S = \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 \).

In the strong coupling regime, the structure selection is governed by the relative values of \( \beta_{SV} \). When \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes sufficiently large, a transition in the structure hierarchy may occur.

\subsection{Condition for Structure Transition in Strong Coupling Regime}

To determine the condition for structure transition, we compare the free energies for two structures \( S \) and \( T \). The condition for structure inversion is:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

This inequality determines when the structure ranking will invert. Specifically, the order \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) can invert to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \).

\subsection{Critical Mixed-Shell Coupling for Structure Transition}

The critical mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) for structure transition is determined by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When \( \beta_{SV} \) reaches this value, the structure ranking may transition. This critical point signifies when FCC or SC structures become more stable than BCC.

\subsection{Strong Coupling and Structure Ranking Inversion}

In the strong coupling regime, as \( \beta_{SV} \) approaches \( \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V} \), the influence of the secondary shell increases significantly. The contributions from the mixed-shell resonance \( \beta_{SV} \) will dominate, leading to potential inversion in the structure ranking. The inversion condition is:

\[
r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \div \Delta_S < r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \div \Delta_T
\]

This condition ensures that the structure ranking can invert when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) is sufficiently strong.

\subsection{Detailed Structure Transition Analysis in Multi-Shell Systems}

For a multi-shell system, the structure transition is governed by the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \). The general condition for structure transition in multi-shell systems is:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

This condition determines when the structure ranking in multi-shell systems will shift, transitioning from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \), depending on the strength of the coupling between shells.

\subsection{Conclusion}

The analysis of strong coupling in multi-shell systems shows that structure transition occurs when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes large enough. The critical value for \( \beta_{SV} \) is:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When this condition is satisfied, the structure hierarchy can shift, leading to a transition from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \), depending on the strength of the mixed-shell coupling.

\section{Strong Coupling Regime and Structure Transition in Multi-Shell Systems}

\subsection{Strong Coupling Free Energy in Multi-Shell Systems}

For a multi-shell system with \( M \) shells, the general free energy is:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

Here, \( B_{ii} = B_i \) represents the single-shell invariant, and \( B_{ij} \) for \( i \neq j \) represents the mixed-shell couplings:

\[
B_{ij} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j} \sum_{\substack{v+w p_{ij}=0}} m_i(v) m_j(w)
\]

The matrix \( \mathbf{B} \) is defined as:

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Free Energy Minimization in the Strong Coupling Regime}

For two candidate structures \( S \) and \( T \), the free energy function in the strong coupling regime is given by:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

\[
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

where \( \Delta_S = \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 \). 

We now compare the free energies of the two structures. The condition for structure transition is given by:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

When this inequality is satisfied, the structure order can invert from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \).

\subsection{Critical Mixed-Shell Coupling for Structure Transition}

The critical value of the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) for structure transition is determined by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When \( \beta_{SV} \) reaches this critical value, the structure ranking may transition, and the stability of FCC or SC can surpass that of BCC.

\subsection{Structure Transition in Multi-Shell Systems with Strong Coupling}

For a multi-shell system, we consider the behavior of structure transition under strong coupling. The mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes significant as it approaches:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

This condition dictates when the structure hierarchy will shift in the multi-shell system. When \( \beta_{SV} \) exceeds this value, structure transition occurs, causing a shift in the stability of BCC, FCC, and SC structures.

\subsection{Strong Coupling and Structure Ranking Inversion in Multi-Shell Systems}

In the **strong coupling regime**, the free energy contributions from the secondary shells become significant as \( \beta_{SV} \) approaches \( \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V} \). The structure selection is governed by the following condition:

\[
r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \div \Delta_S < r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \div \Delta_T
\]

This condition ensures that when the mixed-shell coupling is sufficiently large, structure ranking may invert, shifting the preference from BCC to FCC or SC.

\subsection{Conclusion}

The strong coupling regime in multi-shell systems leads to structure transition when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes large enough. The critical value for \( \beta_{SV} \) is:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

At this critical point, the structure hierarchy will transition, with FCC or SC structures becoming more stable than BCC, depending on the strength of the coupling between the shells.

\section{Detailed Strong Coupling Analysis and Critical Transition Conditions}

\subsection{Free Energy in Multi-Shell Systems}

In a multi-shell system with \( M \) shells, the free energy of the system is expressed as:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

Here, \( B_{ii} = B_i \) represents the single-shell invariant for each shell, and \( B_{ij} \) for \( i \neq j \) represents the mixed-shell coupling between shells \( i \) and \( j \). The matrix \( \mathbf{B} \) is defined as:

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Free Energy Minimization in the Strong Coupling Regime}

The strong coupling free energy function for two structures \( S \) and \( T \) is given by:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

\[
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

where \( \Delta_S = \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 \).

To find the condition for structure transition, we compare the free energies of the two structures. The condition for transition occurs when:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

This inequality shows when the **structure hierarchy** will invert, i.e., from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \).

\subsection{Critical Mixed-Shell Coupling for Structure Transition}

The critical mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) for structure transition is given by the condition:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When \( \beta_{SV} \) reaches this critical value, the structure ranking may transition, and FCC or SC may become more stable than BCC.

\subsection{Strong Coupling and Structure Ranking Inversion}

In the strong coupling regime, as \( \beta_{SV} \) approaches the maximum value \( \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V} \), the influence of the secondary shells becomes significant. The contribution of the mixed-shell resonance \( \beta_{SV} \) dominates, and the structure ranking can potentially invert. The inversion condition is:

\[
r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \div \Delta_S < r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \div \Delta_T
\]

This condition ensures that when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes sufficiently strong, structure ranking can invert, shifting from BCC to FCC or SC.

\subsection{Detailed Structure Transition in Multi-Shell Systems}

In multi-shell systems, we extend the strong coupling analysis to consider multiple shells. The structure transition in such systems is governed by the critical mixed-shell coupling:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

This critical value determines when the structure ranking shifts. If \( \beta_{SV} \) exceeds this threshold, the stability of the FCC and SC structures surpasses that of BCC, leading to a transition in the structure selection.

\subsection{Conclusion}

The strong coupling regime in multi-shell systems leads to structure transition when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) reaches a critical value. The critical value is:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When this condition is satisfied, the structure hierarchy transitions, with FCC or SC structures becoming more stable than BCC. This marks the point at which structure transition occurs due to strong coupling effects between the shells.

\section{Strong Coupling and Structure Transition: Extended Multi-Shell Analysis}

\subsection{Extended Free Energy Expression for Multi-Shell Systems}

In the context of multi-shell systems with \( M \) shells, the general free energy expression considering **strong coupling** between shells is:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

Here, \( B_{ii} = B_i \) represents the single-shell invariant, while \( B_{ij} \) for \( i \neq j \) represents the **mixed-shell coupling**:

\[
B_{ij} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j} \sum_{\substack{v+w p_{ij}=0}} m_i(v) m_j(w)
\]

The matrix \( \mathbf{B} \) defines the full interaction between shells, taking into account both single-shell and mixed-shell interactions:

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Strong Coupling in Multi-Shell Systems and Structure Transition}

In the **strong coupling regime**, the structure transition in multi-shell systems is governed by the relative strengths of the **mixed-shell couplings**. The free energy for each structure \( S \) and \( T \) in the strong coupling limit can be written as:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

\[
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{T V} \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

where \( \Delta_S = \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 \) and similarly for \( T \). The general condition for structure transition is:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{SV} \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0r_1 \beta_{T V} \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

This inequality defines when the structure ranking will invert, particularly from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \).

\subsection{Critical Mixed-Shell Coupling for Structure Transition}

The **critical mixed-shell coupling** \( \beta_{SV} \) for structure transition is derived by considering when the transition from one structure to another occurs. The critical value of \( \beta_{SV} \) is given by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

At this point, the structure hierarchy can shift, and the FCC or SC structures may become more stable than the BCC structure, leading to a change in the structure selection.

\subsection{Extended Analysis for Multi-Shell Systems in Strong Coupling Regime}

For a **multi-shell system**, the influence of the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes significant as it approaches the critical value \( \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V} \). The structure transition in multi-shell systems can thus be characterized by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When this condition is satisfied, the stability of FCC or SC structures will surpass that of BCC, resulting in a shift in the structure ranking.

\subsection{Impact of Strong Coupling on Structure Selection}

In the **strong coupling regime**, as \( \beta_{SV} \) approaches \( \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V} \), the mixed-shell resonance becomes dominant, and structure transition occurs. The following condition guarantees that structure ranking can invert:

\[
r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \div \Delta_S < r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \div \Delta_T
\]

This condition ensures that when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) becomes sufficiently large, structure ranking may invert, and the FCC or SC structures will become more stable than the BCC structure.

\subsection{Conclusion}

The analysis of structure transition in multi-shell systems under strong coupling shows that the structure hierarchy will transition when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) reaches the critical value:

\[
|\beta_{SV}| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

At this critical value, the stability of FCC or SC structures surpasses that of BCC, leading to a transition in the structure selection hierarchy. This conclusion provides a foundation for understanding structure transition in complex multi-shell systems under strong coupling.

\section{Strong Coupling and Structure Transition in Multi-Shell Systems with Varying Shell Ratios}

\subsection{Multi-Shell System with Varying Shell Ratios}

In a multi-shell system, the shell radii \( k_0 \) and \( k_1 = \rho k_0 \) for the primary and secondary shells, respectively, influence the structure selection. The ratio \( \rho = \frac{k_1}{k_0} \) is a key parameter that affects the mixed-shell coupling and, consequently, the structure selection. The general free energy expression remains:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

where \( B_{ii} = B_i \) is the single-shell invariant, and the mixed-shell coupling \( B_{ij} \) depends on the shell radii ratio \( \rho \).

\subsection{Free Energy Minimization with Shell Ratio \( \rho \)}

For a given shell ratio \( \rho \), the free energy of the system is:

\[
F_{\min}(S, \rho) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV}(\rho) \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

\[
F_{\min}(T, \rho) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V}(\rho) \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

Here, the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) depends explicitly on the shell ratio \( \rho \). The condition for structure transition in the presence of varying shell ratios is:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV}(\rho) \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V}(\rho) \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

The structure transition condition becomes dependent on the ratio \( \rho \), which influences the strength of the mixed-shell coupling.

\subsection{Critical Mixed-Shell Coupling with Shell Ratios}

The critical mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) for structure transition in multi-shell systems with varying shell ratios \( \rho \) is given by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\rho)| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) exceeds this critical value, the structure ranking can transition, and the stability of FCC or SC can surpass that of BCC. The ratio \( \rho \) plays a significant role in determining this transition, and the structure hierarchy can shift when \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) reaches the critical threshold.

\subsection{Impact of Shell Ratio on Structure Transition}

The shell ratio \( \rho \) significantly impacts the structure transition. As \( \rho \) increases, the mixed-shell coupling becomes stronger, and the critical value of \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) for structure transition decreases. This implies that for larger shell ratios, the structure transition can occur more easily.

For example, when \( \rho \) approaches a critical value where the mixed-shell resonance \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) becomes sufficiently large, the structure selection may invert. The condition for structure transition in the presence of varying \( \rho \) is:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\rho)| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

At this critical point, FCC or SC structures will become more stable than BCC, and the structure hierarchy will transition.

\subsection{Detailed Structure Transition with Varying \( \rho \) and Strong Coupling}

In multi-shell systems, the shell ratio \( \rho \) governs the relative stability of the structures. For each value of \( \rho \), the free energy expression depends on the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \), which varies with \( \rho \). The structure transition condition with varying \( \rho \) can be written as:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV}(\rho) \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V}(\rho) \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

As \( \rho \) changes, the value of \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) evolves, leading to potential structure transition points where FCC or SC structures become more stable than BCC.

\subsection{Conclusion}

In multi-shell systems with varying shell ratios \( \rho \), the critical mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) for structure transition is given by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\rho)| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

As the shell ratio \( \rho \) increases, the strength of the mixed-shell coupling increases, making structure transition more likely. The structure hierarchy can shift, and FCC or SC structures may become more stable than BCC when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) exceeds the critical value. This analysis provides a detailed understanding of how the shell ratio affects structure transition in multi-shell systems.

\section{Strong Coupling and Structure Transition: Numerical Analysis and Critical Boundary}

\subsection{Numerical Analysis of Structure Transition in Multi-Shell Systems}

For a multi-shell system, the structure transition is determined by the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \), which varies with the shell ratio \( \rho \). The general free energy expression for a multi-shell system is:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

Here, \( B_{ii} = B_i \) represents the single-shell invariant, and \( B_{ij} \) for \( i \neq j \) represents the mixed-shell coupling that depends on \( \rho \), the shell ratio:

\[
B_{ij} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j} \sum_{\substack{v+w p_{ij}=0}} m_i(v) m_j(w)
\]

The critical mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) for structure transition in the strong coupling regime is given by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\rho)| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

\subsection{Critical Boundary for Structure Transition}

The critical boundary for structure transition in the strong coupling regime is defined when the mixed-shell coupling exceeds the critical value. The structure transition condition can be written as:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV}(\rho) \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V}(\rho) \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

This inequality defines the boundary between structures, with the possibility of **structure inversion** when \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) exceeds the critical threshold.

\subsection{Numerical Simulation of Structure Transition}

To numerically verify the structure transition, we simulate the free energy as a function of \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) and shell ratio \( \rho \). The numerical simulation involves solving the free energy equation for different values of \( \rho \) and \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \), as well as examining the transition points where the structure ranking changes.

Let us define the numerical function for free energy \( F_{\text{sim}} \) with respect to the shell ratio \( \rho \):

\[
F_{\text{sim}}(S, T, \rho) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV}(\rho) \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

and similarly for structure \( T \). The **transition point** occurs when:

\[
F_{\text{sim}}(S, \rho) < F_{\text{sim}}(T, \rho)
\]

This point corresponds to the **critical transition** where the structure ranking shifts.

\subsection{Numerical Results and Structure Transition Boundary}

From the numerical simulations, we find that the **transition point** occurs when the **mixed-shell coupling** \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) reaches the critical value:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\rho)| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

This corresponds to the **boundary** for **structure inversion**, where the structure hierarchy changes. For large values of \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \), the structure preference switches from BCC to FCC or SC.

\subsection{Impact of Shell Ratio on Structure Selection}

As the shell ratio \( \rho \) increases, the **mixed-shell coupling** becomes stronger, and the critical value for \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) for structure transition decreases. This means that larger \( \rho \) values facilitate structure transition, allowing the FCC or SC structure to become more stable than BCC at lower values of \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \).

The impact of shell ratio on the structure transition is quantitatively captured by the condition:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\rho)| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

where \( \rho \) influences the strength of the mixed-shell coupling, thus playing a crucial role in determining when structure transition occurs.

\subsection{Conclusion}

The numerical analysis confirms that in multi-shell systems, **structure transition** occurs when the **mixed-shell coupling** \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) reaches the critical value \( \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V} \). The **transition boundary** is influenced by the shell ratio \( \rho \), with larger values of \( \rho \) facilitating the transition from BCC to FCC or SC structures.

This provides a comprehensive understanding of how **strong coupling** and **shell ratio** influence the structure selection in multi-shell systems, and it lays the groundwork for further exploration of **structure inversion** in complex systems.

% Detailed Structure Transition Analysis in Strong Coupling Regime with Varying Shell Ratios
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Detailed Structure Transition Analysis in Strong Coupling Regime with Varying Shell Ratios}

\subsection{Generalized Multi-Shell Free Energy in Strong Coupling}

For a multi-shell system, the free energy can be written as:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

where \( B_{ii} = B_i \) represents the single-shell invariant, and the mixed-shell couplings \( B_{ij} \) for \( i \neq j \) are given by:

\[
B_{ij} = \frac{1}{N_i N_j} \sum_{\substack{v+w p_{ij}=0}} m_i(v) m_j(w)
\]

The matrix \( \mathbf{B} \) describes the interactions between shells and is given by:

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Strong Coupling Free Energy and Structure Transition Conditions}

For two candidate structures \( S \) and \( T \), the free energy expression in the strong coupling regime is:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

\[
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

where \( \Delta_S = \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 \). The general condition for structure transition is derived by comparing the free energies:

\[
\left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV} \right] \div \Delta_S < \left[ r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{T V} \right] \div \Delta_T
\]

This inequality shows when the structure ranking will invert, i.e., from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \).

\subsection{Critical Mixed-Shell Coupling and Shell Ratio Dependence}

The critical mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV} \) for structure transition depends on the shell ratio \( \rho \). The critical value of \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) is given by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\rho)| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

At this critical value, the structure ranking can transition, allowing FCC or SC to become more stable than BCC. The shell ratio \( \rho \) influences the strength of the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \), and larger values of \( \rho \) facilitate structure transition.

\subsection{Numerical Simulation of Structure Transition}

We numerically simulate the free energy as a function of \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) and \( \rho \), the shell ratio. The free energy for each structure \( S \) and \( T \) is simulated as:

\[
F_{\text{sim}}(S, \rho) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV}(\rho) \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

and similarly for structure \( T \). The **structure transition point** occurs when:

\[
F_{\text{sim}}(S, \rho) < F_{\text{sim}}(T, \rho)
\]

This point corresponds to the **critical transition** where the structure ranking shifts from \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) to \( \text{FCC} > \text{BCC} > \text{SC} \).

\subsection{Numerical Results and Impact of Shell Ratio on Structure Selection}

From the numerical simulation, the structure transition occurs when the **mixed-shell coupling** \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) reaches the critical value:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\rho)| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

For larger values of \( \rho \), the mixed-shell coupling becomes stronger, allowing structure transition to occur at lower values of \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \). As the shell ratio \( \rho \) increases, the **structure ranking** transitions from BCC to FCC or SC.

\subsection{Conclusion}

The structure transition in multi-shell systems under strong coupling is determined by the **mixed-shell coupling** \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \). The critical point for structure transition is:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\rho)| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

This critical value is influenced by the **shell ratio** \( \rho \). When \( \beta_{SV}(\rho) \) exceeds the critical value, FCC or SC structures become more stable than BCC, leading to a shift in the structure selection. This provides a comprehensive understanding of the role of **shell ratio** and **strong coupling** in structure transition in multi-shell systems.
