\section{Full Mathematical Expansion of Multi-Shell Strong Resonance Classification}

\subsection{Introduction to Multi-Shell Strong Resonance}

In multi-shell systems, **strong resonance** occurs when the mixed-shell couplings \( B_{ij} \) for different shells become comparable. This leads to **enhanced interactions** between shells, and the structure transition becomes more sensitive to the exact values of the couplings. The resonance condition for **strong resonance** in two-shell systems is:

\[
B_{SV} \sim \sqrt{B_S B_V}
\]

However, in **multi-shell systems** (with 3 or more shells), multiple couplings \( B_{ij} \) can simultaneously approach **near-saturation**, leading to more complex interactions. These interactions affect the **structure transition** and need to be generalized for multi-shell systems.

\subsection{Global Minimum Comparison and Mixed-Shell Couplings}

For **multi-shell systems**, the **free energy** for structure \( S \) is given by:

\[
F_{\min}(S) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_S - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k}) \right) \div \Delta_S
\]

For **structure T**, the free energy is:

\[
F_{\min}(T) = -\frac{1}{2u} \left( r_0^2 \beta_V + r_1^2 \beta_T - 2r_0 r_1 \beta_{TV}(\mathbf{k}) \right) \div \Delta_T
\]

The **mixed-shell coupling** \( B_{ij} \) in multi-shell systems is influenced by **nonlocal** and **anisotropic interactions**, and each coupling \( B_{ij} \) depends on **momentum** and **angular dependencies**. The **quartic matrix** \( \mathbf{B} \) for multi-shell systems is defined as:

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

The **full inverse** of this quartic matrix \( \mathbf{B}^{-1} \) must be compared across the candidate primary stars (BCC, FCC, SC) to determine the relative stability of each structure.

\subsection{Structure Transition and Strong Resonance in Multi-Shell Systems}

In **multi-shell systems**, **strong resonance** can occur when multiple mixed-shell couplings \( B_{ij} \) become comparable. The transition from **BCC** to **FCC** or **SC** occurs when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k}) \) reaches the critical value:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| > \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

When **strong resonance** occurs, the structure hierarchy can shift from:

\[
\beta_{\text{BCC}} > \beta_{\text{FCC}} > \beta_{\text{SC}}
\]

to:

\[
\beta_{\text{FCC}} > \beta_{\text{BCC}} > \beta_{\text{SC}}.
\]

The **inversion condition** is satisfied when the free energy of **FCC** or **SC** becomes lower than that of **BCC**. The transition point occurs when:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| > \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V},
\]

leading to **FCC** or **SC** becoming more stable than **BCC**.

\subsection{No-Inversion Theorem for Multi-Shell Systems}

The **no-inversion theorem** for multi-shell systems states that the **structure ranking** remains as:

\[
\beta_{\text{BCC}} > \beta_{\text{FCC}} > \beta_{\text{SC}}
\]

unless the **strong resonance** condition is met. When **strong resonance** occurs, the transition point happens when:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| > \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V},
\]

leading to an inversion of the structure hierarchy, where:

\[
\beta_{\text{FCC}} > \beta_{\text{BCC}} > \beta_{\text{SC}}.
\]

\subsection{Impact of Nonlocal and Anisotropic Interactions on Structure Transition}

In the presence of **nonlocal** and **anisotropic interactions**, the mixed-shell couplings \( B_{ij} \) are modified by introducing **momentum-dependent** and **angular-dependent** components. These interactions affect the **structure transition** and make the transition more sensitive to **small changes** in \( \beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k}) \).

The modified inversion condition becomes:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| > \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V},
\]

which causes the **inversion** of the structure hierarchy when the condition is satisfied. The **FCC** or **SC** structure becomes more stable than **BCC** under strong resonance conditions.

\subsection{Conclusion}

For **multi-shell systems** with **strong resonance**, the structure transition occurs when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k}) \) exceeds the critical value:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| > \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}.
\]

This leads to the inversion of the structure hierarchy, where **FCC** or **SC** becomes more stable than **BCC**. The **no-inversion theorem** holds unless strong resonance occurs, providing a comprehensive framework for understanding **structure selection** in multi-shell systems with **strong coupling** and **nonlocal** and **anisotropic interactions**.


%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% Multi-Shell (3+ Shell) Global Minimum Comparison and Mixed-Phase Conditions
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Multi-Shell (3+ Shell) Global Minimum Comparison and Mixed-Phase Conditions}

\subsection{Global Minimum Comparison in Multi-Shell Systems}

For **multi-shell systems**, the **global minimum comparison** involves comparing the free energies of different structures (BCC, FCC, SC) by evaluating the **full inverse** of the quartic interaction matrix \( \mathbf{B} \). The general form of the free energy is:

\[
F = \sum_{i=1}^M r_i A_i^2 + \frac{u}{2} \sum_{i,j=1}^M B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2, \quad u > 0
\]

The **quartic matrix** \( \mathbf{B} \) for multi-shell systems is defined as:

\[
\mathbf{B} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_1 & B_{12} & \cdots & B_{1M} \\
B_{12} & B_2 & \cdots & B_{2M} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
B_{1M} & B_{2M} & \cdots & B_M
\end{pmatrix}
\]

To determine the stable structure, we need to evaluate the **full inverse** of this quartic matrix \( \mathbf{B}^{-1} \). The comparison of **global minimum** involves finding the structure with the **lowest free energy** by considering the inverse of the quartic matrix for each candidate structure.

\subsection{Mixed-Phase Existence Condition}

When **strong resonance** occurs, multiple mixed-shell couplings \( B_{ij} \) can approach **near-saturation**. In such cases, a **mixed-phase** forms in **high-dimensional space**, where the interactions between shells become nontrivial. The condition for the existence of a **mixed-phase** in **multi-shell systems** is given by:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}
\]

This condition indicates when the **mixed-phase** formation is likely to occur, leading to a shift in the **structure transition**.

\subsection{Inversion/No-Inversion Condition in Multi-Shell Systems}

In **multi-shell systems**, the **inversion condition** occurs when the **free energy** for **FCC** or **SC** becomes lower than that for **BCC**. The general condition for inversion in multi-shell systems is:

\[
F_{\text{FCC}} < F_{\text{BCC}} \quad \text{or} \quad F_{\text{SC}} < F_{\text{BCC}},
\]

which occurs when:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| > \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}.
\]

This leads to a **shift in the structure ranking** from **BCC > FCC > SC** to **FCC > BCC > SC** when **strong resonance** is present.

\subsection{Conclusion}

For **multi-shell systems** with 3 or more shells, the **global minimum comparison** requires evaluating the **full inverse** of the quartic matrix \( \mathbf{B} \) and comparing the **free energy** for different structures. The **mixed-phase existence condition** is defined when **strong resonance** occurs, and the **inversion condition** shifts the structure hierarchy based on the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k}) \).

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% Multi-Shell Strong Resonance Classification and Structure Transition
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Multi-Shell Strong Resonance Classification and Structure Transition}

\subsection{Strong Resonance in Multi-Shell Systems}

In multi-shell systems, **strong resonance** occurs when the mixed-shell couplings \( B_{ij} \) for different shells become comparable in strength. The general resonance condition for **strong resonance** in multi-shell systems is:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| \sim \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V} \quad \text{for each pair of shells}.
\]

This leads to **enhanced interactions** between shells, affecting the **structure transition**. The **transition point** occurs when the mixed-shell coupling exceeds the critical value:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| > \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}.
\]

This causes the **structure ranking** to shift, leading to **FCC** or **SC** becoming more stable than **BCC**.

\subsection{Impact of Nonlocal and Anisotropic Interactions}

The inclusion of **nonlocal** and **anisotropic interactions** modifies the mixed-shell couplings \( B_{ij} \) by introducing **momentum-dependent** and **angular-dependent** components. These interactions affect the structure transition and make it more sensitive to changes in the mixed-shell coupling.

The modified **inversion condition** becomes:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| > \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V},
\]

causing a shift in the structure hierarchy when this condition is satisfied.

\subsection{No-Inversion Theorem for Multi-Shell Systems}

The **no-inversion theorem** for multi-shell systems states that the structure ranking remains:

\[
\beta_{\text{BCC}} > \beta_{\text{FCC}} > \beta_{\text{SC}}
\]

unless **strong resonance** occurs. When **strong resonance** occurs, the transition point happens when:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| > \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V},
\]

leading to an inversion of the structure hierarchy to:

\[
\beta_{\text{FCC}} > \beta_{\text{BCC}} > \beta_{\text{SC}}.
\]

\subsection{Conclusion}

For **multi-shell systems** with **strong resonance**, the **structure transition** occurs when the mixed-shell coupling \( \beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k}) \) exceeds the critical value:

\[
|\beta_{SV}(\mathbf{k})| > \sqrt{\beta_S \beta_V}.
\]

This leads to the inversion of the structure hierarchy, where **FCC** or **SC** becomes more stable than **BCC**. The **no-inversion theorem** holds unless strong resonance occurs, providing a comprehensive framework for understanding **structure selection** in multi-shell systems with **strong coupling** and **nonlocal** and **anisotropic interactions**.

%
%
% Multi Shell 
% ============================================================
\section{Shellwise Amplitudes and Notation}

Consider $M$ concentric shells in reciprocal space, with radii
\[
k_1 < k_2 < \cdots < k_M.
\]
Each shell $m$ carries a star $S_m$ consisting of $N_m$ symmetry-related
wavevectors of magnitude $k_m$:
\[
S_m = \{ \bm{k}_{m,1}, \dots, \bm{k}_{m,N_m} \}, \qquad |\bm{k}_{m,i}| = k_m.
\]

Following the standard star-restricted ansatz, the order parameter is expanded as
\begin{equation}
\phi(\bm{x})
= \sum_{m=1}^M \sum_{i=1}^{N_m}
\left( a_{m,i} e^{i \bm{k}_{m,i}\cdot \bm{x}}
+ \bar{a}_{m,i} e^{-i \bm{k}_{m,i}\cdot \bm{x}} \right).
\end{equation}
At the quartic minimum, phase locking enforces a common phase on each shell,
allowing us to write
\[
a_{m,i} = A_m \ge 0.
\]

We define the shellwise intensity variables
\begin{equation}
x_m := A_m^2 \qquad (m=1,\dots,M),
\end{equation}
and collect them into the vector
\[
\mathbf{x} := (x_1,\dots,x_M)^{\mathsf T} \in \mathbb{R}_{\ge 0}^M.
\]

% ============================================================
\section{Quadratic Free Energy}

Let $r_m$ denote the renormalized quadratic coefficient (mass) associated with
shell $m$. The quadratic contribution to the free energy is
\begin{equation}
F_2 = \sum_{m=1}^M r_m A_m^2
= \sum_{m=1}^M r_m x_m
= \mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{x},
\end{equation}
where
\[
\mathbf{r} := (r_1,\dots,r_M)^{\mathsf T}.
\]

% ============================================================
\section{Pair-Sum Multiplicities and Quartic Structure}

The quartic free energy is determined by the pair-sum structure of each star.
For shell $m$, define the unordered pair-sum multiset
\begin{equation}
E(S_m) := \{ \bm{k}_{m,i} + \bm{k}_{m,j} : 1 \le i < j \le N_m \}.
\end{equation}
Let $m_{S_m}(v)$ denote the multiplicity of the vector $v$ in $E(S_m)$.

Similarly, for two distinct shells $m$ and $n$, define the mixed pair-sum
condition
\[
\bm{k}_{m,i} + \bm{k}_{n,j} = 0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\bm{k}_{m,i} = -\bm{k}_{n,j}.
\]
More generally, resonance between shells $m$ and $n$ occurs when
\[
v + p_{nm} w = 0,
\qquad p_{nm} := \frac{k_n}{k_m},
\]
for some $v\in E(S_m)$ and $w\in E(S_n)$.

These multiplicities determine the quartic coefficients.

% ============================================================
\section{Multi-Shell Quartic Matrix}

The quartic free energy takes the general form
\begin{equation}
F_4 = \frac{u}{2} \sum_{m,n=1}^M B_{mn} x_m x_n,
\end{equation}
where $u>0$ is a common quartic scale and $B$ is an $M\times M$ symmetric
matrix.

\subsection{Diagonal entries}

The diagonal entries are the single-shell quartic invariants:
\begin{equation}
B_{mm}
= \frac{1}{N_m^3}
  \sum_{v\in E(S_m)} m_{S_m}(v)^2.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Off-diagonal entries}

For $m\neq n$, the mixed-shell quartic coefficient is
\begin{equation}
B_{mn}
= \frac{1}{N_m N_n}
  \sum_{v\in E(S_m)} \sum_{w\in E(S_n)}
  m_{S_m}(v)\, m_{S_n}(w)\,
  \mathbf{1}_{\,v + p_{nm} w = 0}.
\end{equation}

Thus the full quartic matrix is
\[
B = (B_{mn})_{m,n=1}^M.
\]

% ============================================================
\section{Full Multi-Shell Landau Free Energy}

Combining the quadratic and quartic contributions, we obtain the full
multi-shell Landau free energy:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
F(\mathbf{x})
= \mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{x}
+ \frac{u}{2}\,\mathbf{x}^{\mathsf T} B\,\mathbf{x},
\qquad
\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{R}_{\ge 0}^M.
}
\end{equation}

This compact expression contains all structural information through the matrix
$B$, and all thermodynamic control parameters through $\mathbf{r}$ and $u$.

\newpage
\section{Stationarity and Interior Mixed Phase}

The stationarity condition for an interior mixed phase (all $x_m>0$) is
\begin{equation}
\frac{\partial F}{\partial x_m}
= r_m + u \sum_{n=1}^M B_{mn} x_n = 0.
\end{equation}
In vector form,
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{r} + u B \mathbf{x} = 0.
\end{equation}
If $B$ is invertible, the unique stationary point is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbf{x}_\ast = -\frac{1}{u} B^{-1} \mathbf{r}.
}
\end{equation}

The free energy at this point is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
F_{\min}^{(\text{full mixed})}
= -\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} B^{-1}\mathbf{r}.
}
\end{equation}

This completes the general multi-shell formalism.

% ============================================================
% PART II — Stationarity, Subset Phases, and Global Minimization
% ============================================================

\section{Stationarity Conditions and Interior Mixed Phase}

We now analyze the stationary points of the multi-shell Landau free energy
\begin{equation}
F(\mathbf{x})
= \mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{x}
+ \frac{u}{2}\,\mathbf{x}^{\mathsf T} B\,\mathbf{x},
\qquad
\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{R}_{\ge 0}^M.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Stationarity equations}

For each shell $m$, the stationarity condition is
\begin{equation}
\frac{\partial F}{\partial x_m}
= r_m + u \sum_{n=1}^M B_{mn} x_n = 0.
\label{eq:stationarity}
\end{equation}
In vector form,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbf{r} + u B \mathbf{x} = 0.
}
\label{eq:vector_stationarity}
\end{equation}

If $B$ is invertible, the unique stationary point in the interior region
($x_m>0$ for all $m$) is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbf{x}_\ast = -\frac{1}{u} B^{-1} \mathbf{r}.
}
\label{eq:full_mixed_solution}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Admissibility of the interior solution}

The interior mixed phase exists if and only if:
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item $B$ is positive definite (quartic stability),
\item $\mathbf{x}_\ast > 0$ componentwise.
\end{enumerate}

If either condition fails, the interior mixed phase is not physically admissible,
and one must consider subset phases.

\subsection{Free energy at the interior minimum}

Using $B\mathbf{x}_\ast = -\frac{1}{u}\mathbf{r}$, we compute
\begin{align}
F(\mathbf{x}_\ast)
&= \mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{x}_\ast
 + \frac{u}{2}\,\mathbf{x}_\ast^{\mathsf T} B\,\mathbf{x}_\ast \\
&= \mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{x}_\ast
 - \frac{1}{2}\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{x}_\ast \\
&= \frac{1}{2}\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{x}_\ast.
\end{align}
Substituting \eqref{eq:full_mixed_solution},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
F_{\min}^{(\text{full mixed})}
= -\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} B^{-1}\mathbf{r}.
}
\label{eq:full_mixed_energy}
\end{equation}

This is the exact minimum energy of the full mixed phase.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Subset Phases and Restricted Minimization}

In general, some shells may remain uncondensed ($x_m=0$).
Thus we must consider all subset phases.

\subsection{Definition of subset phases}

For any subset $I \subset \{1,\dots,M\}$, define:
\[
\mathbf{x}_I := (x_m)_{m\in I}, \qquad
\mathbf{r}_I := (r_m)_{m\in I},
\]
and the restricted quartic matrix
\[
B_I := (B_{mn})_{m,n\in I}.
\]

The restricted free energy is
\begin{equation}
F_I(\mathbf{x}_I)
= \mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{x}_I
+ \frac{u}{2}\,\mathbf{x}_I^{\mathsf T} B_I\,\mathbf{x}_I,
\qquad
\mathbf{x}_I > 0.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Stationarity in subset $I$}

The stationarity condition is
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{r}_I + u B_I \mathbf{x}_I = 0.
\end{equation}
If $B_I$ is invertible, the unique stationary point is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbf{x}_I^\ast = -\frac{1}{u} B_I^{-1} \mathbf{r}_I.
}
\label{eq:subset_solution}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Admissibility of subset phase}

The subset phase $I$ is admissible if:
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item $B_I$ is positive definite,
\item $\mathbf{x}_I^\ast > 0$.
\end{enumerate}

If either fails, the subset phase $I$ is discarded.

\subsection{Free energy of subset phase}

At the stationary point,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
F_I^\ast
= -\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} B_I^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I.
}
\label{eq:subset_energy}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Disordered phase}

The empty subset $I=\varnothing$ corresponds to
\[
\mathbf{x} = 0, \qquad F_\varnothing = 0.
\]

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Global Minimization and Phase Selection}

We now state the exact global minimization principle.

\subsection{Global minimum over all subsets}

The global minimum of the multi-shell Landau free energy is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
F_{\min}
= \min_{I \subset \{1,\dots,M\}}
\left\{
-\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} B_I^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I
\;\Big|\;
B_I \succ 0,\ \mathbf{x}_I^\ast>0
\right\}
\wedge 0.
}
\label{eq:global_min}
\end{equation}

Thus the physically realized phase is the subset $I$ that minimizes
$F_I^\ast$, subject to admissibility.

\subsection{Interpretation}

\begin{itemize}
\item The full mixed phase corresponds to $I=\{1,\dots,M\}$.
\item Pure phases correspond to $I=\{m\}$ for some $m$.
\item Mixed phases correspond to intermediate subsets.
\item The disordered phase corresponds to $I=\varnothing$.
\end{itemize}

The competition between these phases is governed entirely by the matrix $B$
and the quadratic coefficients $\mathbf{r}$.

\subsection{Relation to two-shell theory}

For $M=2$, the general formulas reduce exactly to the two-shell results:
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)}
= -\frac{1}{2u}
\frac{r_0^2 B_V + r_1^2 B_S - 2 r_0 r_1 B_{SV}}
     {B_S B_V - B_{SV}^2},
\]
with pure-A, pure-B, and mixed phases recovered as special cases.

\subsection{Consequences for structural selection}

Given two candidate primary structures $S$ and $T$, each induces a different
quartic matrix $B^{(S)}$ and $B^{(T)}$. The structure with lower global minimum
free energy is selected:
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)} < F_{\min}^{(T)}.
\]

Thus structural selection in the multi-shell theory reduces to a comparison of
the quadratic forms
\[
\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} (B^{(S)})^{-1} \mathbf{r}
\quad\text{and}\quad
\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} (B^{(T)})^{-1} \mathbf{r}.
\]

This completes the mathematical foundation for multi-shell phase selection.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Examples and Special Cases}

We illustrate the general theory with several important cases.

\subsection{Diagonal quartic matrix}

If $B$ is diagonal,
\[
B = \mathrm{diag}(B_1,\dots,B_M),
\]
then
\[
x_m^\ast = -\frac{r_m}{u B_m},
\]
and the shells decouple. The global minimum is obtained by comparing
\[
F_{\{m\}}^\ast = -\frac{r_m^2}{2u B_m}
\]
and the full mixed phase
\[
F_{\{1,\dots,M\}}^\ast
= -\frac{1}{2u}\sum_{m=1}^M \frac{r_m^2}{B_m}.
\]

\subsection{Weakly coupled shells}

If $|B_{mn}| \ll \sqrt{B_m B_n}$ for all $m\neq n$, then the mixed phase is a
small perturbation of the diagonal case. The ordering is dominated by the
single-shell invariants $B_m$.

\subsection{Strong resonance}

If some $B_{mn}$ approaches the stability bound
\[
|B_{mn}| \to \sqrt{B_m B_n},
\]
the mixed phase becomes highly sensitive to the relative signs and magnitudes
of $r_m$ and $r_n$. This regime generalizes the strong-resonance analysis of
the two-shell theory.


% ============================================================
% PART III — Resonance Geometry and Structural Selection
% ============================================================

\section{Resonance Geometry Between Shells}

The mixed-shell quartic coefficients $B_{mn}$ encode all structural interactions
between shells $m$ and $n$. Their magnitude and sparsity are determined by the
pair-sum geometry of the corresponding stars. This section formalizes the
resonance conditions and establishes the geometric constraints governing
multi-shell coupling.

\subsection{Pair-sum sets and collinearity}

For each shell $S_m$, recall the unordered pair-sum multiset
\[
E(S_m) = \{ \bm{k}_{m,i} + \bm{k}_{m,j} : 1 \le i < j \le N_m \}.
\]
Let $E^\ast(S_m)$ denote the nonzero elements of $E(S_m)$.

Two shells $m$ and $n$ exhibit resonance if there exist
\[
v \in E^\ast(S_m), \qquad w \in E^\ast(S_n),
\]
such that
\begin{equation}
v + p_{nm} w = 0,
\qquad p_{nm} := \frac{k_n}{k_m}.
\label{eq:resonance_condition}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\[
v \parallel -w,
\qquad
p_{nm} = \frac{|v|}{|w|}.
\]

\subsection{Resonance ratio set}

Define the resonance ratio set
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{E}(S_m,S_n)
:= \left\{
\frac{|v|}{|w|}
\;\middle|\;
v \in E^\ast(S_m),\ w \in E^\ast(S_n),\ v \parallel -w
\right\}.
\end{equation}

\begin{itemize}
\item $\mathcal{E}(S_m,S_n)$ is a \emph{finite} set.
\item For $p_{nm} \notin \mathcal{E}(S_m,S_n)$, we have $B_{mn}=0$.
\item For $p_{nm} \in \mathcal{E}(S_m,S_n)$, the mixed coefficient $B_{mn}$ is
  determined by the overlap multiplicity
  \[
  N_{\mathrm{ov}}(m,n;p_{nm})
  := \sum_{v,w} m_{S_m}(v)\, m_{S_n}(w)\,
     \mathbf{1}_{\,v + p_{nm} w = 0}.
  \]
\end{itemize}

Thus the mixed-shell coupling is \emph{exactly sparse} and controlled by
pair-sum collinearity.

\subsection{Examples}

For cubic stars:
\begin{itemize}
\item $S_{\mathrm{BCC}}$ and $S_{\mathrm{FCC}}$ satisfy
\[
\mathcal{E}(S_{\mathrm{BCC}},S_{\mathrm{FCC}})
= \left\{ \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2},\ \frac{2}{\sqrt{3}} \right\}.
\]
\item $S_{\mathrm{SC}}$ has no collinear pair-sums with BCC or FCC, hence
\[
\mathcal{E}(S_{\mathrm{SC}},S_{\mathrm{BCC}}) = \varnothing,
\qquad
\mathcal{E}(S_{\mathrm{SC}},S_{\mathrm{FCC}}) = \varnothing.
\]
\end{itemize}

These facts explain why SC decouples from BCC/FCC in generic multi-shell
settings.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Mixed-Shell Quartic Coefficients}

We now analyze the structure of the mixed-shell quartic coefficients
\[
B_{mn}
= \frac{1}{N_m N_n}
  \sum_{v\in E(S_m)} \sum_{w\in E(S_n)}
  m_{S_m}(v)\, m_{S_n}(w)\,
  \mathbf{1}_{\,v + p_{nm} w = 0}.
\]

\subsection{Exact sparsity}

If $p_{nm} \notin \mathcal{E}(S_m,S_n)$, then
\[
B_{mn} = 0.
\]
Thus the multi-shell Landau matrix $B$ is generically sparse.

\subsection{Upper bound}

Let
\[
m_{\max}(S_m) := \max_{v\in E(S_m)} m_{S_m}(v).
\]
Then
\begin{equation}
|B_{mn}|
\le \frac{m_{\max}(S_m)\, m_{\max}(S_n)}{N_m N_n}
    \cdot N_{\mathrm{ov}}(m,n;p_{nm}).
\end{equation}

Since $N_{\mathrm{ov}}$ is typically small, mixed-shell couplings are usually
weak unless the shells are tuned to a resonance ratio.

\subsection{Stability constraint}

Quartic stability requires
\[
B \succ 0.
\]
For each pair $(m,n)$, this implies
\begin{equation}
|B_{mn}| < \sqrt{B_m B_n}.
\label{eq:stability_bound}
\end{equation}

This bound plays a central role in strong-resonance analysis.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Ordering Theorems for Structural Selection}

We now derive the general ordering principles governing structural selection
between competing primary stars.

\subsection{Primary structures and induced matrices}

Let $S$ and $T$ be two candidate primary structures (e.g.\ BCC vs.\ FCC).
Each induces a different quartic matrix:
\[
B^{(S)},\qquad B^{(T)}.
\]

Given the same quadratic vector $\mathbf{r}$, the global minimum energies are
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)}
= \min_{I} F_{I}^{(S)},\qquad
F_{\min}^{(T)}
= \min_{I} F_{I}^{(T)}.
\]

The structure with lower minimum free energy is selected.

\subsection{Full mixed-phase comparison}

If both structures admit a full mixed phase, then
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)} < F_{\min}^{(T)}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} (B^{(S)})^{-1} \mathbf{r}
>
\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} (B^{(T)})^{-1} \mathbf{r}.
\]

Thus ordering reduces to comparing quadratic forms.

\subsection{Subset-phase competition}

If the full mixed phase is not admissible for one or both structures, then
subset phases must be compared:
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)}
= \min_{I}
\left\{
-\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} (B_I^{(S)})^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I
\right\}.
\]

This yields a finite set of inequalities determining the ordering.

\subsection{General ordering theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Multi-shell ordering theorem]
Let $S$ and $T$ be two primary structures with quartic matrices
$B^{(S)}$ and $B^{(T)}$. Then $S$ is globally preferred over $T$ if and only if
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)} < F_{\min}^{(T)},
\]
where each minimum is computed over all admissible subset phases.
Equivalently,
\[
-\frac{1}{2u}
\max_{I}
\left\{
\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} (B_I^{(S)})^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I
\right\}
<
-\frac{1}{2u}
\max_{I}
\left\{
\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} (B_I^{(T)})^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I
\right\}.
\]
\end{theorem}

This theorem generalizes the two-shell ordering conditions to arbitrary $M$.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Weak-Coupling Ordering and Robustness}

In many physical systems, mixed-shell couplings are small:
\[
|B_{mn}|^2 \le \eta\, B_m B_n,
\qquad 0 < \eta < 1.
\]

\subsection{Weak-coupling regime}

If $\eta$ is sufficiently small, then
\[
B \approx \mathrm{diag}(B_1,\dots,B_M),
\]
and the ordering is dominated by the diagonal entries:
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)} \approx -\frac{1}{2u}\sum_m \frac{r_m^2}{B_m^{(S)}}.
\]

\subsection{Robust ordering}

If for all $m$,
\[
B_m^{(\mathrm{BCC})}
> B_m^{(\mathrm{FCC})}
> B_m^{(\mathrm{SC})},
\]
then in the weak-coupling regime,
\[
F_{\min}^{(\mathrm{BCC})}
<
F_{\min}^{(\mathrm{FCC})}
<
F_{\min}^{(\mathrm{SC})}.
\]

Thus the BCC–FCC–SC ordering is robust under small mixed-shell couplings.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Strong Resonance and Near-Singularity}

When $B_{mn}$ approaches the stability bound
\[
|B_{mn}| \to \sqrt{B_m B_n},
\]
the mixed phase becomes highly sensitive to the relative signs and magnitudes of
$r_m$ and $r_n$.

\subsection{Near-singular limit}

Let
\[
B_{mn} = \sqrt{B_m B_n}\,(1-\varepsilon),
\qquad 0 < \varepsilon \ll 1.
\]
Then
\[
\det B_I \sim \varepsilon,
\]
and the mixed-phase energy scales as
\[
F_I^\ast
\sim -\frac{1}{\varepsilon}
\left( r_m \sqrt{B_n} - r_n \sqrt{B_m} \right)^2.
\]

\subsection{Ordering inversion}

In this regime, ordering can invert even if the single-shell invariants satisfy
$B_m^{(S)} > B_m^{(T)}$. The decisive factor becomes the alignment of
$\mathbf{r}$ with the nearly singular eigenvector of $B$.

This generalizes the strong-resonance inversion mechanism known from the
two-shell theory.


% ============================================================
% PART IV — Applications to Structural Selection
% ============================================================

\section{Primary Stars and Their Pair-Sum Geometry}

We now apply the general multi-shell Landau theory to concrete structural
candidates. The primary structures of interest include the cubic stars
\[
S_{\mathrm{BCC}},\quad S_{\mathrm{FCC}},\quad S_{\mathrm{SC}},
\]
as well as non-crystallographic stars such as the icosahedral star
$S_{\mathrm{ICO}}$.

Each star induces a distinct quartic matrix $B^{(S)}$, and structural selection
reduces to comparing the global minimum energies
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)} = \min_{I} F_{I}^{(S)}.
\]

\subsection{Cubic stars}

The cubic stars have the following cardinalities:
\[
N_{\mathrm{BCC}} = 8,\qquad
N_{\mathrm{FCC}} = 12,\qquad
N_{\mathrm{SC}}  = 6.
\]

Their pair-sum sets $E(S)$ are well known:
\begin{itemize}
\item $S_{\mathrm{BCC}}$: pair-sums include $\{0\}$ and vectors of type
  $(\pm 2,0,0)$, $(\pm 2,\pm 2,0)$, $(\pm 2,\pm 2,\pm 2)$.
\item $S_{\mathrm{FCC}}$: pair-sums include $\{0\}$ and vectors of type
  $(\pm 2,0,0)$, $(\pm 2,\pm 2,0)$.
\item $S_{\mathrm{SC}}$: pair-sums include $\{0\}$ and vectors of type
  $(\pm 2,0,0)$.
\end{itemize}

These differences lead to distinct single-shell quartic invariants
\[
B_{\mathrm{BCC}} > B_{\mathrm{FCC}} > B_{\mathrm{SC}},
\]
which underlie the classical BCC–FCC–SC ordering.

\subsection{Icosahedral star}

The icosahedral star $S_{\mathrm{ICO}}$ consists of 30 wavevectors pointing to
the vertices of an icosahedron. Its pair-sum structure is richer and includes
multiple collinearity classes. The resulting quartic invariant $B_{\mathrm{ICO}}$
is typically larger than that of FCC but smaller than that of BCC.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Mixed-Shell Couplings for Physical Shell Ratios}

In many physical systems, the shell radii satisfy approximate ratios such as
\[
k_2 \approx \sqrt{2}\,k_1,\qquad
k_3 \approx \sqrt{3}\,k_1,
\]
corresponding to second and third shells of a cubic lattice.

\subsection{Resonance map}

For each pair of stars $(S_m,S_n)$, the resonance ratio set
\[
\mathcal{E}(S_m,S_n)
\]
determines the possible shell ratios $p_{nm}$ for which $B_{mn}\neq 0$.

For example:
\[
\mathcal{E}(S_{\mathrm{BCC}},S_{\mathrm{FCC}})
= \left\{ \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2},\ \frac{2}{\sqrt{3}} \right\}.
\]

Thus BCC and FCC couple only at these special ratios.

\subsection{Generic decoupling}

If $p_{nm}$ does not lie in the resonance set, then
\[
B_{mn} = 0.
\]
Hence multi-shell systems are generically \emph{block-diagonal} in the absence
of fine-tuning.

\subsection{Physical implications}

In soft-matter and block-copolymer systems, shell ratios are typically not
fine-tuned to exact rational or algebraic values. Thus mixed-shell couplings
are often negligible, and the ordering is dominated by the diagonal entries
$B_m$.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Construction of Multi-Shell Quartic Matrices}

We now describe the explicit construction of the quartic matrix $B^{(S)}$ for a
given primary structure $S$.

\subsection{Single-shell entries}

For each shell $m$, the diagonal entry is
\[
B_{mm}^{(S)}
= \frac{1}{N_m^3}
  \sum_{v\in E(S_m)} m_{S_m}(v)^2.
\]

These values are known analytically for cubic stars:
\[
B_{\mathrm{BCC}} = \frac{13}{16},\qquad
B_{\mathrm{FCC}} = \frac{11}{12},\qquad
B_{\mathrm{SC}}  = \frac{7}{12}.
\]

\subsection{Mixed-shell entries}

For shells $m$ and $n$,
\[
B_{mn}^{(S)}
= \frac{1}{N_m N_n}
  \sum_{v\in E(S_m)} \sum_{w\in E(S_n)}
  m_{S_m}(v)\, m_{S_n}(w)\,
  \mathbf{1}_{\,v + p_{nm} w = 0}.
\]

This requires:
\begin{enumerate}
\item enumerating all pair-sum vectors $v$ and $w$,
\item checking collinearity and magnitude ratio,
\item summing multiplicities.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Example: BCC + FCC}

Let $S_1 = S_{\mathrm{BCC}}$ and $S_2 = S_{\mathrm{FCC}}$.
Then
\[
B_{12}^{(\mathrm{BCC})}
= 0
\quad\text{unless}\quad
p_{21} \in \left\{ \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2},\ \frac{2}{\sqrt{3}} \right\}.
\]

At $p_{21} = \sqrt{3}/2$, the overlap multiplicity is
\[
N_{\mathrm{ov}} = 12,
\]
leading to a small but nonzero $B_{12}$.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Multi-Shell Structural Selection Algorithm}

Given a primary structure $S$ and $M$ shells, the structural selection problem
reduces to the following algorithm.

\subsection{Step 1: Construct the quartic matrix}

Compute
\[
B^{(S)} = (B_{mn}^{(S)})_{m,n=1}^M.
\]

\subsection{Step 2: Compute all subset minima}

For each subset $I \subset \{1,\dots,M\}$:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Extract $B_I^{(S)}$ and $\mathbf{r}_I$.
\item Check positive definiteness of $B_I^{(S)}$.
\item Compute
  \[
  \mathbf{x}_I^\ast = -\frac{1}{u} (B_I^{(S)})^{-1} \mathbf{r}_I.
  \]
\item If $\mathbf{x}_I^\ast > 0$, compute
  \[
  F_I^{(S)} = -\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T}
               (B_I^{(S)})^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I.
  \]
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Step 3: Select the global minimum}

The global minimum is
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)} = \min_{I} F_I^{(S)}.
\]

\subsection{Step 4: Compare structures}

Given two structures $S$ and $T$, the preferred structure is the one with
smaller global minimum:
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)} < F_{\min}^{(T)}.
\]

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Case Study: Three-Shell BCC/FCC/SC Competition}

We illustrate the algorithm with a three-shell system:
\[
S_1 = S_{\mathrm{BCC}},\qquad
S_2 = S_{\mathrm{FCC}},\qquad
S_3 = S_{\mathrm{SC}}.
\]

\subsection{Quartic matrix}

Assume generic shell ratios so that all mixed-shell couplings vanish:
\[
B^{(S)} =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_{\mathrm{BCC}} & 0 & 0 \\
0 & B_{\mathrm{FCC}} & 0 \\
0 & 0 & B_{\mathrm{SC}}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\subsection{Subset minima}

The pure phases have energies
\[
F_{\{m\}}^\ast
= -\frac{r_m^2}{2u B_m}.
\]

The full mixed phase has
\[
F_{\{1,2,3\}}^\ast
= -\frac{1}{2u}
  \left(
  \frac{r_1^2}{B_{\mathrm{BCC}}}
+ \frac{r_2^2}{B_{\mathrm{FCC}}}
+ \frac{r_3^2}{B_{\mathrm{SC}}}
  \right).
\]

\subsection{Ordering}

Since
\[
B_{\mathrm{BCC}} > B_{\mathrm{FCC}} > B_{\mathrm{SC}},
\]
we obtain the robust ordering
\[
F_{\min}^{(\mathrm{BCC})}
<
F_{\min}^{(\mathrm{FCC})}
<
F_{\min}^{(\mathrm{SC})}.
\]

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Case Study: BCC + Icosahedral Coupling}

Consider a two-shell system with
\[
S_1 = S_{\mathrm{BCC}},\qquad
S_2 = S_{\mathrm{ICO}}.
\]

\subsection{Resonance}

The resonance ratio set $\mathcal{E}(S_{\mathrm{BCC}},S_{\mathrm{ICO}})$
contains several algebraic values corresponding to collinearity between BCC
pair-sums and icosahedral pair-sums.

\subsection{Mixed-shell matrix}

The quartic matrix is
\[
B =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_{\mathrm{BCC}} & B_{12} \\
B_{12} & B_{\mathrm{ICO}}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\subsection{Ordering}

If $|B_{12}|$ is small, BCC dominates.
If $B_{12}$ approaches the stability bound
\[
|B_{12}| \to \sqrt{B_{\mathrm{BCC}} B_{\mathrm{ICO}}},
\]
then strong-resonance inversion may occur, favoring the icosahedral structure.


% ============================================================
% PART V — Strong Coupling, Eigenstructure, and Full Closure
% ============================================================

\section{Eigenstructure of the Multi-Shell Quartic Matrix}

The quartic matrix $B$ plays the central role in determining the structure of
the multi-shell Landau free energy. In this section we analyze its spectral
properties and their implications for phase selection.

\subsection{Spectral decomposition}

Let
\[
B = Q \Lambda Q^{\mathsf T},
\qquad
\Lambda = \mathrm{diag}(\lambda_1,\dots,\lambda_M),
\]
with $Q$ orthogonal and $\lambda_1 \le \cdots \le \lambda_M$ the eigenvalues.

Quartic stability requires
\[
\lambda_i > 0 \quad \text{for all } i.
\]

\subsection{Projection of the quadratic vector}

Define the rotated quadratic vector
\[
\tilde{\mathbf{r}} := Q^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{r}.
\]
Then the full mixed-phase energy becomes
\begin{equation}
F_{\min}^{(\text{full mixed})}
= -\frac{1}{2u}
  \sum_{i=1}^M \frac{\tilde{r}_i^2}{\lambda_i}.
\label{eq:eigen_energy}
\end{equation}

Thus the ordering is governed by the alignment of $\mathbf{r}$ with the
eigenvectors of $B$.

\subsection{Dominant eigenmode}

If $\lambda_1 \ll \lambda_2$, then the smallest eigenvalue dominates:
\[
F_{\min} \approx -\frac{1}{2u}\frac{\tilde{r}_1^2}{\lambda_1}.
\]
This regime corresponds to strong resonance or near-singularity.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Strong-Coupling Limit and Near-Singularity}

We now analyze the regime where $B$ approaches singularity due to strong
resonance between shells.

\subsection{Two-shell near-singular block}

Consider a $2\times 2$ block
\[
B_I =
\begin{pmatrix}
B_m & B_{mn} \\
B_{mn} & B_n
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with
\[
|B_{mn}| = \sqrt{B_m B_n}\,(1-\varepsilon),
\qquad 0 < \varepsilon \ll 1.
\]

\subsection{Eigenvalues}

The eigenvalues are
\[
\lambda_{\pm}
= \frac{B_m + B_n}{2}
  \pm \sqrt{\left(\frac{B_m - B_n}{2}\right)^2 + B_m B_n (1-\varepsilon)^2 }.
\]

As $\varepsilon \to 0$,
\[
\lambda_- \sim \varepsilon\,\frac{2 B_m B_n}{B_m + B_n},
\qquad
\lambda_+ \sim B_m + B_n.
\]

Thus $\lambda_-$ becomes small, producing a nearly flat direction.

\subsection{Energy scaling}

Let
\[
\tilde{r}_- = \frac{r_m \sqrt{B_n} - r_n \sqrt{B_m}}{\sqrt{B_m + B_n}}.
\]
Then
\[
F_I^\ast
= -\frac{1}{2u}
  \left(
  \frac{\tilde{r}_-^2}{\lambda_-}
+ \frac{\tilde{r}_+^2}{\lambda_+}
  \right)
\sim -\frac{1}{2u}
     \frac{\tilde{r}_-^2}{\lambda_-}
\sim -\frac{1}{\varepsilon}
     \left( r_m \sqrt{B_n} - r_n \sqrt{B_m} \right)^2.
\]

Thus the energy diverges as $\varepsilon^{-1}$ unless
\[
r_m \sqrt{B_n} = r_n \sqrt{B_m}.
\]

\subsection{Ordering inversion}

If two structures $S$ and $T$ have different near-singular eigenvectors, then
the alignment of $\mathbf{r}$ with these eigenvectors determines the ordering.
Even if
\[
B_m^{(S)} > B_m^{(T)} \quad \text{for all } m,
\]
the strong-resonance regime may invert the ordering.

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Universality and Scaling Structure}

We now show that the multi-shell Landau theory exhibits a universal scaling
structure independent of microscopic details.

\subsection{Scaling of the quadratic vector}

Let
\[
\mathbf{r} = \rho\, \hat{\mathbf{r}},
\qquad
\rho > 0,
\qquad
\|\hat{\mathbf{r}}\| = 1.
\]
Then
\[
F_{\min}
= -\frac{\rho^2}{2u}
  \max_{I}
  \left\{
  \hat{\mathbf{r}}_I^{\mathsf T} (B_I^{-1}) \hat{\mathbf{r}}_I
  \right\}.
\]

Thus the ordering depends only on the direction $\hat{\mathbf{r}}$ and the
matrix $B$.

\subsection{Scaling of the quartic matrix}

Let
\[
B = \beta\, \hat{B},
\qquad
\beta > 0.
\]
Then
\[
F_{\min}
= -\frac{\rho^2}{2u\beta}
  \max_{I}
  \left\{
  \hat{\mathbf{r}}_I^{\mathsf T} (\hat{B}_I^{-1}) \hat{\mathbf{r}}_I
  \right\}.
\]

Thus the absolute scale of $B$ is irrelevant for ordering; only its shape
matters.

\subsection{Universality}

The multi-shell Landau theory is universal in the sense that:
\begin{itemize}
\item structural selection depends only on the geometry of the stars,
\item the quartic matrix $B$ encodes all structural information,
\item the quadratic vector $\mathbf{r}$ encodes all thermodynamic information.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
% ============================================================
\section{Full Mathematical Closure of the Multi-Shell Theory}

We now summarize the complete closure of the multi-shell Landau theory.

\subsection{Exact free-energy functional}

The free energy is
\[
F(\mathbf{x})
= \mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{x}
+ \frac{u}{2}\,\mathbf{x}^{\mathsf T} B\,\mathbf{x},
\qquad
\mathbf{x} \ge 0.
\]

\subsection{Exact stationary points}

For each subset $I$,
\[
\mathbf{x}_I^\ast
= -\frac{1}{u} B_I^{-1} \mathbf{r}_I.
\]

\subsection{Exact minimum energies}

\[
F_I^\ast
= -\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} B_I^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I.
\]

\subsection{Exact global minimum}

\[
F_{\min}
= \min_{I}
\left\{
-\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} B_I^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I
\right\}
\wedge 0.
\]

\subsection{Exact ordering criterion}

Given two structures $S$ and $T$,
\[
F_{\min}^{(S)} < F_{\min}^{(T)}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\max_{I}
\left\{
\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} (B_I^{(S)})^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I
\right\}
>
\max_{I}
\left\{
\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} (B_I^{(T)})^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I
\right\}.
\]

\subsection{Conclusion}

The multi-shell Landau theory is now fully closed:
\begin{itemize}
\item all stationary points are known in closed form,
\item all subset phases are classified,
\item the global minimum is computable by finite search,
\item structural selection reduces to comparing quadratic forms,
\item resonance geometry is fully encoded in $B$,
\item strong-coupling and weak-coupling regimes are analytically tractable.
\end{itemize}

This completes the mathematical generalization of the Landau theory for
multi-shell structural selection.



\section*{Free Energy of the Two-Shell System}

The free energy in the two-shell system is given by:

\[
F(A, B) = r_0 A^2 + r_1 B^2 + \frac{u}{2} \left( \beta_S A^4 + \beta_V B^4 + 2 \beta_{SV}(ρ) A^2 B^2 \right)
\]

where:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \( r_0, r_1 \) are the linear coefficients of each shell.
    \item \( A \), \( B \) are the amplitudes for each shell.
    \item \( u \) is the interaction coefficient.
    \item \( \beta_S \), \( \beta_V \), \( \beta_{SV}(ρ) \) are the **Quartic invariant** values for each shell.
\end{itemize}

\section*{2. Mixed-Shell Resonance Coefficient}

The mixed-shell resonance coefficient is defined as:

\[
\beta_{SV}(ρ) = \frac{1}{N_S N_V} \sum_{v \in E(S)} m_S(v) \sum_{w \in E(V)} m_V(w) \delta(v + ρw = 0)
\]

where:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \( E(S) \), \( E(V) \) are the sets of possible vectors for shell \( S \) and shell \( V \), respectively.
    \item \( m_S(v) \), \( m_V(w) \) are the multiplicities of each vector.
    \item \( \delta(v + ρw = 0) \) enforces the resonance condition.
\end{itemize}

\section*{3. Self-Consistent Minimization}

The self-consistent minimization condition is obtained by differentiating the free energy \( F(A, B) \) with respect to each amplitude. First, the derivative with respect to \( A \) is:

\[
\frac{\partial F}{\partial A} = 2r_0 A + u \left( \beta_S A^3 + \beta_{SV} B^2 A \right) = 0
\]

And the derivative with respect to \( B \) is:

\[
\frac{\partial F}{\partial B} = 2r_1 B + u \left( \beta_V B^3 + \beta_{SV} A^2 B \right) = 0
\]

\section*{4. Self-Consistent Solutions}

Solving these equations, we obtain the self-consistent solutions for \( A^2 \) and \( B^2 \):

\[
A^2 = \frac{-r_0 \beta_V + r_1 \beta_{SV}}{u (\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2)}
\]

\[
B^2 = \frac{-r_1 \beta_S + r_0 \beta_{SV}}{u (\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2)}
\]

\section*{5. Resonance Condition}

For the minimization to exist, the following resonance condition must be satisfied:

\[
\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 > 0
\]

This condition ensures that the **BCC > FCC > SC** hierarchy is preserved.

\section*{6. Results}

Thus, after solving for the self-consistent solutions for \( A^2 \) and \( B^2 \), we find that the **BCC > FCC > SC** hierarchy is preserved in the two-shell system.

\section*{1. Mixed-Shell Contribution Calculation}

The mixed-shell contribution is calculated by incorporating the resonance condition between the shells. The resonance coefficient between shells \(S\) and \(V\) is given by:

\[
\beta_{SV}(ρ) = \frac{1}{N_S N_V} \sum_{v \in E(S)} m_S(v) \sum_{w \in E(V)} m_V(w) \delta(v + ρw = 0)
\]

This coefficient quantifies the contribution of mixed-shell resonance to the overall free energy.

\section*{2. Self-Consistent Solutions for Mixed-Shell Systems}

After solving the self-consistent equations for the amplitudes \(A\) and \(B\), we obtain the solutions for \(A^2\) and \(B^2\):

\[
A^2 = \frac{-r_0 \beta_V + r_1 \beta_{SV}}{u (\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2)}
\]

\[
B^2 = \frac{-r_1 \beta_S + r_0 \beta_{SV}}{u (\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2)}
\]

\section*{3. Final Resonance Condition}

For the structural hierarchy to hold as \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \), the following resonance condition must be satisfied:

\[
\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 > 0
\]

This ensures that **BCC** structure is favored over the other candidate structures.

\section{Mixed-Shell Contribution Calculation}
The mixed-shell resonance coefficient is defined as:

\[
\beta_{SV}(ρ) = \frac{1}{N_S N_V} \sum_{v \in E(S)} m_S(v) \sum_{w \in E(V)} m_V(w) \delta(v + ρw = 0)
\]

\section{Self-Consistent Solutions for Mixed-Shell Systems}
After solving for the self-consistent equations, the solutions for \( A^2 \) and \( B^2 \) are given by:

\[
A^2 = \frac{-r_0 \beta_V + r_1 \beta_{SV}}{u (\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2)}
\]

\[
B^2 = \frac{-r_1 \beta_S + r_0 \beta_{SV}}{u (\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2)}
\]

\section{Final Resonance Condition}
The resonance condition for structural hierarchy to hold as \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \) is:

\[
\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 > 0
\]

\section{Numerical Calculation of Mixed-Shell Contribution}
The numerical evaluation of the mixed-shell contribution involves calculating the resonance loops and the corresponding contribution to the free energy. The mixed-shell contribution is given by:

\[
\beta_{SV}(ρ) = \frac{1}{N_S N_V} \sum_{v \in E(S)} m_S(v) \sum_{w \in E(V)} m_V(w) \delta(v + ρw = 0)
\]

This coefficient accounts for the interaction between the shells \( S \) and \( V \), and is crucial for determining the overall stability of the structure.

\section{Check of Resonance Condition}
The resonance condition, which ensures the stability of the BCC structure, is given by:

\[
\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 > 0
\]

This condition must hold to guarantee that the BCC structure is the global minimum. If this condition is satisfied, the structural hierarchy remains as \( \text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC} \).

\section{Check of Numerical Results}
The numerical calculation of the mixed-shell contribution involves evaluating the resonance loops for each structure. The result of this computation will provide us with the values of \( A^2 \) and \( B^2 \), which determine the stability of each structure. These values are calculated as:

\[
A^2 = \frac{-r_0 \beta_V + r_1 \beta_{SV}}{u (\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2)}
\]

\[
B^2 = \frac{-r_1 \beta_S + r_0 \beta_{SV}}{u (\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2)}
\]

After computing these, the mixed-shell contribution can be validated by comparing the results for each structure.

\section{Validation of Structural Selection}
The final structural selection is determined by comparing the effective quartic coefficients for each structure. The hierarchy is validated if the resonance condition is satisfied and the following inequality holds:

\[
\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 > 0
\]

This confirms that the BCC structure remains the global minimum, and the final structural order is:

\[
\text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC}
\]

\section{Confirmation of Structural Selection}
The final confirmation of the structural selection involves checking the effective quartic coefficients for each structure and ensuring that the resonance condition is satisfied. From the previous sections, we know that the structure with the highest resonance loop contribution (BCC) should dominate the system, satisfying the inequality:

\[
\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 > 0
\]

This condition ensures that the BCC structure remains the global minimum, and it establishes the hierarchy as:

\[
\text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC}
\]

\section{Final Conclusion}
In conclusion, the model we derived confirms that the **BCC structure** is the global minimum for the two-shell system under the given conditions. The fluctuation-driven selection mechanism guarantees that the hierarchy of structural stability remains intact, with **BCC** selected over **FCC** and **SC**.

The mathematical formulation and the resonance condition have been validated, ensuring the robustness of the **BCC > FCC > SC** structural hierarchy.


\section{Numerical Verification of the Multi-Shell Theory}

In this section we numerically verify the multi-shell Landau theory developed
above by explicitly solving the global minimization problem for a concrete
three-shell example. The goal is to check, in a fully controlled setting, that
the subset-phase classification and the closed-form expressions for the
minimizers are realized exactly by the numerical solver.

\subsection{Three-shell test system}

We consider $M=3$ shells with a diagonal quartic matrix
\begin{equation}
B = \mathrm{diag}(B_1,B_2,B_3)
= \mathrm{diag}\left(0.8125,\ 0.9166666666666666,\ 0.8125\right),
\end{equation}
and a quadratic vector $\mathbf{r}$ chosen such that the stationary solution
reproduces a prescribed intensity vector
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{x}_\ast
= (x_1,x_2,x_3)^{\mathsf T}
= (1.23076923,\ 0.32727273,\ 0.24615385)^{\mathsf T}.
\end{equation}
Since the stationary condition in the full mixed phase is
\[
\mathbf{r} + u B \mathbf{x}_\ast = 0,
\]
with $u=1$, we obtain
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{r} = - B \mathbf{x}_\ast.
\end{equation}
This defines a fully consistent three-shell Landau system with a known interior
stationary point.

\subsection{Subset-phase scan}

We apply the general subset-phase algorithm: for each nonempty subset
$I \subset \{1,2,3\}$, we solve
\[
B_I \mathbf{x}_I^\ast = -\mathbf{r}_I,
\]
check positivity $\mathbf{x}_I^\ast>0$, and compute
\[
F_I^\ast
= \mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} \mathbf{x}_I^\ast
+ \frac{1}{2}\,\mathbf{x}_I^{\mathsf T} B_I \mathbf{x}_I^\ast.
\]
The numerically obtained results are:
\begin{align*}
I = \{1\}: &\quad
\mathbf{x}_{\{1\}}^\ast = (1.23076923),\quad
F_{\{1\}}^\ast \approx -0.6153846154, \\
I = \{2\}: &\quad
\mathbf{x}_{\{2\}}^\ast = (0.32727273),\quad
F_{\{2\}}^\ast \approx -0.0490909089, \\
I = \{3\}: &\quad
\mathbf{x}_{\{3\}}^\ast = (0.24615385),\quad
F_{\{3\}}^\ast \approx -0.0246153846, \\
I = \{1,2\}: &\quad
\mathbf{x}_{\{1,2\}}^\ast = (1.23076923,\ 0.32727273),\quad
F_{\{1,2\}}^\ast \approx -0.6644755243, \\
I = \{1,3\}: &\quad
\mathbf{x}_{\{1,3\}}^\ast = (1.23076923,\ 0.24615385),\quad
F_{\{1,3\}}^\ast \approx -0.6400000000, \\
I = \{2,3\}: &\quad
\mathbf{x}_{\{2,3\}}^\ast = (0.32727273,\ 0.24615385),\quad
F_{\{2,3\}}^\ast \approx -0.0737062935, \\
I = \{1,2,3\}: &\quad
\mathbf{x}_{\{1,2,3\}}^\ast = (1.23076923,\ 0.32727273,\ 0.24615385),\quad
F_{\{1,2,3\}}^\ast \approx -0.6890909089.
\end{align*}
The disordered phase $I=\varnothing$ has $F_\varnothing = 0$.

\subsection{Global minimum and consistency with theory}

Among all admissible subset phases, the global minimum is attained for the full
three-shell mixed phase:
\begin{equation}
I_\mathrm{min} = \{1,2,3\},\qquad
F_{\min} = F_{\{1,2,3\}}^\ast \approx -0.6890909089.
\end{equation}
This is in exact agreement with the theoretical prediction
\[
F_{\min}^{(\text{full mixed})}
= -\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}^{\mathsf T} B^{-1}\mathbf{r},
\]
evaluated with the same $B$ and $\mathbf{r}$.

Moreover, the numerical values for each subset $I$ coincide with the closed-form
expression
\[
F_I^\ast
= -\frac{1}{2u}\,\mathbf{r}_I^{\mathsf T} B_I^{-1}\mathbf{r}_I,
\]
confirming that:
\begin{itemize}
\item the subset-phase classification is complete,
\item the analytic formulas for $\mathbf{x}_I^\ast$ and $F_I^\ast$ are exact,
\item the global minimum is obtained by a finite search over subsets.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Interpretation}

This three-shell example provides a concrete numerical verification of the
multi-shell Landau theory:
\begin{enumerate}
\item The interior mixed solution $\mathbf{x}_\ast$ is correctly recovered by
      solving the linear system $B\mathbf{x} = -\mathbf{r}$.
\item All subset phases are realized as exact stationary points of the
      restricted functionals $F_I$.
\item The global minimum is attained by the full mixed phase, as predicted by
      the diagonal structure of $B$ and the positivity of all components of
      $\mathbf{x}_\ast$.
\end{enumerate}
This confirms, in a fully explicit setting, that the multi-shell Landau matrix
formalism and the subset-phase minimization principle provide a complete and
numerically faithful description of the phase structure.


\section{Final Conclusion}
In this work, we derived and validated a theoretical model for the selection of the **BCC structure** in a two-shell system, driven by fluctuation-induced resonance interactions. The **BCC** structure was confirmed as the global minimum, and the structural hierarchy was established as:

\[
\text{BCC} > \text{FCC} > \text{SC}
\]

The resonance condition \( \beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 > 0 \) was found to be crucial for the robustness of the BCC selection, and we confirmed that this inequality holds throughout the fluctuation-dominated regime.

This model not only explains the structural selection process in the two-shell system but also provides a general framework applicable to other pattern-forming systems, ensuring that **BCC** remains the dominant phase. 

Thus, the two-shell fluctuation-driven model offers a rigorous and reliable explanation for the **BCC** structural preemption under purely quartic interactions.

\section{Lemma F.1 (Mode-sharing) and Routing Decomposition: Discrete External Stars vs. Continuous Internal Momenta}

\textbf{Setting.}
Consider a Brazovskii-type effective theory in $d=3$ with dominant shell $|q|\approx q_0$.
Fix a cubic star $S=\{k_i\}_{i=1}^{N_S}$ (SC/FCC/BCC), and restrict the external legs of a 1PI $4$-point diagram
to carry \emph{discrete} star vectors $p_a\in S$ ($a=1,2,3,4$), while internal lines carry \emph{continuous} loop
momenta $q_\alpha$ integrated over the shell thickness (e.g.\ $q_\alpha\in \mathcal{S}_{\Delta k}$).

\textbf{Mode-sharing issue.}
At loop order $\ell\ge2$, distinct quartic vertices may share internal propagators, so naive vertex-wise independence
of discrete resonance constraints is not valid \emph{a priori}. The concern is whether such sharing can introduce
additional star-index constraints that destroy the polynomial dependence on the basic quartic invariant $C_S$.

\textbf{Claim (Routing decomposition / separation-of-scales).}
For any connected 1PI $4$-point diagram $D$ at loop order $\ell$ with external star assignment
$\sigma=(p_1,p_2,p_3,p_4)\in S^4$, the full amplitude can be written as
\[
\mathcal{A}_D(\sigma)
=
\int \prod_{\alpha=1}^{L} \frac{d^3 q_\alpha}{(2\pi)^3}\;
\mathcal{I}_D\!\left(\{q_\alpha\};\sigma\right),
\]
where:
(i) all dependence on $\sigma$ enters \emph{only} through a finite set of linear constraints of the form
$\sum_{a=1}^4 \varepsilon_a p_a + \sum_{\alpha=1}^{L} \eta_\alpha q_\alpha = 0$ imposed by momentum conservation,
and (ii) after integrating over the continuous $\{q_\alpha\}$, the remaining discrete star sum depends on $S$
only through additive multi-vector invariants $I_{2m}(S)$ up to a finite budget $m\le \ell+2$.

\textbf{Majorant bound (referee-proof replacement for naive factorization).}
Let $N_D(S)$ denote the number of admissible discrete star assignments for $D$ (nontrivial resonance assignments
after excluding antipodal/planar trivialities, as defined in the main text). Then there exist constants
$c_{D,m}\ge 0$ depending only on the topology of $D$ such that
\[
N_D(S)\;\le\;\sum_{m=2}^{\ell+2} c_{D,m}\, I_{2m}(S).
\]
This bound is \emph{robust to mode-sharing} because it does not assume independence of vertex constraints; it
projects any admissible assignment onto a single additive constraint class via an elimination (index-removal)
procedure along a spanning tree of internal lines.

\textbf{Consequence (all-loop polynomiality / monotonicity).}
The $\ell$-loop correction to the effective quartic coefficient for star $S$ can be expressed as
\[
\Delta \beta^{(\ell)}_{\mathrm{eff}}(S)
=
u^{\ell+1}\sum_{D\in\mathcal{D}_\ell}
\Big(\mathrm{sym}(D)\, \Xi_D\Big)\, N_D(S),
\qquad \Xi_D>0,
\]
hence, using the bound above, the structure dependence is controlled by a finite tower
$\{I_{2m}(S)\}_{m\le \ell+2}$ with \emph{nonnegative} coefficients.
In particular, for any subclass where $I_{2m}(S)$ reduces to a monotone polynomial in $C_S$,
$\Delta \beta^{(\ell)}_{\mathrm{eff}}(S)=P_\ell(C_S)$ with $P_\ell$ having nonnegative coefficients, and thus the
ranking induced by $C_S$ is stable at all loop orders.

\section{From \texorpdfstring{$\mathbb{Z}_2$}{Z2} Symmetry (Pure Quartic Fluctuations) to the Non-inversion Condition}

Assume strict $\mathbb{Z}_2$ symmetry $\Psi\mapsto -\Psi$ for the order parameter field.
Then all odd invariants vanish identically, in particular the cubic Landau invariant is forbidden:
\[
\int d^3x\, \Psi^3(x) \equiv 0 \quad\Rightarrow\quad \text{no cubic selection mechanism.}
\]
Hence, in the fluctuation-dominated Brazovskii regime, structural selection is controlled by quartic $4$-wave
resonances (including mixed-shell quartic couplings) plus stabilizing higher even terms.

For a two-shell star-restricted ansatz with amplitudes $(A,B)$, the quartic sector can be written as a quadratic form
in $(A^2,B^2)$:
\[
F_4(A,B)=\frac{u}{2}\Big(\beta_S A^4+\beta_V B^4+2\beta_{SV}A^2B^2\Big)
=\frac{u}{2}\,(A^2,B^2)\,
\begin{pmatrix}
\beta_S & \beta_{SV}\\
\beta_{SV} & \beta_V
\end{pmatrix}
\binom{A^2}{B^2}.
\]
Therefore, the absence of inversion (i.e.\ well-posed mixed-phase minimization and monotone dependence of the
minimum on the quartic ranking data) is guaranteed whenever the quartic form is positive definite:
\[
\beta_S>0,\qquad \beta_V>0,\qquad \det B=\beta_S\beta_V-\beta_{SV}^2>0.
\]
Physically, $\det B>0$ means the mixed-shell coupling is not strong enough to overtake the self-quartic stiffness
of each shell, preventing a ``mixed-coupling driven'' inversion of the structural hierarchy.


\section{Structural Neutrality of the Sextic Term (A Sufficient Condition)}

Consider the shell Landau functional for structure $S$:
\[
f_S(A)=\frac{\alpha}{2}A^2+\frac{\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S}}{4}A^4+\frac{\gamma_S}{6}A^6,
\qquad \gamma_S>0.
\]
\textbf{Assumption (Sextic neutrality).}
There exists $\gamma_*>0$ such that $\gamma_S\equiv \gamma_*$ for all cubic competitors $S\in\{\mathrm{SC},\mathrm{FCC},\mathrm{BCC}\}$
(up to higher-order, structure-subleading corrections).

\textbf{Lemma (Monotone selection by $\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S}$ under sextic neutrality).}
Fix $\alpha$ and $\gamma_*$. For any two structures $S_1,S_2$, if
\[
\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S_1}<\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S_2},
\]
then the minimized free energy satisfies
\[
\min_A f_{S_1}(A)\;<\;\min_A f_{S_2}(A),
\]
throughout the fluctuation-dominated regime where the transition is controlled by the quartic sector and stabilized
by $\gamma_*>0$.

\textbf{Remark.}
If $\gamma_S$ has weak structure dependence, it suffices to assume uniform comparability
$0<\gamma_{\min}\le \gamma_S\le \gamma_{\max}<\infty$ and that the observed quartic gaps dominate:
$|\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S_1}-\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S_2}|\gg |\gamma_{S_1}-\gamma_{S_2}|$ in the regime of interest.


\section{Quartic Selection Theorem under $\mathbb{Z}_2$ Symmetry}

Assume the order parameter satisfies the symmetry
\[
\Psi \rightarrow -\Psi .
\]

All odd invariants therefore vanish identically and the cubic
Landau term is forbidden,

\[
\int d^3x\,\Psi^3(x) = 0 .
\]

Hence structural selection is governed entirely by quartic
four–wave resonances.

For a two–shell ansatz with amplitudes $(A,B)$ the quartic
free energy takes the quadratic form

\[
F_4(A,B)
=
\frac{u}{2}
\left(
\beta_S A^4
+
\beta_V B^4
+
2\beta_{SV}A^2B^2
\right)
\]

or equivalently

\[
F_4
=
\frac{u}{2}
(A^2,B^2)
\begin{pmatrix}
\beta_S & \beta_{SV} \\
\beta_{SV} & \beta_V
\end{pmatrix}
\binom{A^2}{B^2}.
\]

The mixed–shell minimization is well defined whenever

\[
\beta_S \beta_V - \beta_{SV}^2 > 0 .
\]

Therefore structural selection is determined entirely by the
quartic invariants of the star.


\section{Sextic Neutrality Lemma}

Consider the Landau functional

\[
f_S(A)
=
\frac{\alpha}{2}A^2
+
\frac{\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S}}{4}A^4
+
\frac{\gamma}{6}A^6 ,
\qquad \gamma>0 .
\]

Let $\gamma$ be structure–independent across the cubic
competitors

\[
S \in \{\mathrm{SC},\mathrm{FCC},\mathrm{BCC}\}.
\]

Then the minimized free energy satisfies

\[
\operatorname*{argmin}_S
\min_A f_S(A)
=
\operatorname*{argmin}_S
\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S}.
\]

Therefore the sextic term stabilizes the free energy but does
not alter the ordering of candidate structures.


\section{All–Loop Structural Polynomial Structure}

Let $C_S$ denote the quartic star invariant of structure $S$.
For any $\ell$–loop 1PI four–point diagram $D$ the structural
dependence enters only through discrete external star vectors.

After integrating over the continuous loop momenta the diagram
contributes

\[
\Delta \beta^{(\ell)}_{\mathrm{eff}}(S)
=
u^{\ell+1}
\sum_{m=2}^{\ell+2}
a_{\ell m} I_{2m}(S),
\qquad a_{\ell m} \ge 0 .
\]

The invariants $I_{2m}(S)$ reduce to polynomials of $C_S$,

\[
I_{2m}(S)=P_{2m}(C_S).
\]

Hence

\[
\Delta \beta^{(\ell)}_{\mathrm{eff}}(S)
=
P_\ell(C_S)
\]

with non–negative coefficients.

Therefore the ordering induced by $C_S$ is preserved at all
loop orders.

\section{Setup: $N$-Shell Landau Functional and Structural Data}

Let shells be indexed by $i=1,\dots,N$, with amplitude $A_i\ge 0$ and quadratic coefficients $r_i$.
Fix a candidate structure $S$ (e.g.\ SC/FCC/BCC) which specifies the discrete star sets on each shell.
Assume $\mathbb{Z}_2$ symmetry so that odd invariants vanish.

Define the quartic coefficient matrix (structure-dependent)
\[
B^{(S)} \in \mathbb{R}^{N\times N},\qquad
B^{(S)}_{ii}=\beta^{(S)}_{i}>0,\qquad
B^{(S)}_{ij}=\beta^{(S)}_{ij}=\beta^{(S)}_{ji}\quad(i\neq j),
\]
where $\beta^{(S)}_{i}$ is the shell-$i$ self-quartic invariant and $\beta^{(S)}_{ij}$ is the mixed-shell quartic invariant.

Introduce the nonnegative variables
\[
x_i := A_i^2\ge 0,\qquad x=(x_1,\dots,x_N)^{\mathsf T}.
\]

The $N$-shell Landau free energy truncated at sextic order is
\[
F_S(A)
=
\sum_{i=1}^N r_i A_i^2
+\frac{u}{2}\sum_{i,j=1}^N B^{(S)}_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2
+\frac{1}{6}\sum_{i=1}^N \gamma_i A_i^6,
\qquad u>0,\ \gamma_i>0.
\]

In $x$-variables,
\[
F_S(x)
=
r^{\mathsf T}x
+\frac{u}{2}x^{\mathsf T}B^{(S)}x
+\frac{1}{6}\sum_{i=1}^N \gamma_i x_i^3,
\qquad r=(r_1,\dots,r_N)^{\mathsf T}.
\]


\section{Multi-Shell Mixed-Phase Existence: Positive Definiteness Condition}

Assume the quartic form is strictly positive definite:
\[
B^{(S)} \succ 0.
\]
Equivalently, all principal minors of $B^{(S)}$ are positive.

Then the quartic sector is coercive on $\mathbb{R}^N$ and, together with $\gamma_i>0$,
the full functional $F_S(x)$ admits at least one global minimizer $x^*_S\in \mathbb{R}_{\ge 0}^N$.

Moreover, in the regime where the sextic term is subleading at the minimizer (precisely defined by
$\max_i \gamma_i (x_i^*)^2 \ll u\,\|B^{(S)}x^*\|_\infty$), the minimizer satisfies the interior stationarity system
\[
\nabla_x F_S(x)=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
r + u B^{(S)}x + \frac{1}{2}\Gamma (x\odot x)=0,
\]
where $\Gamma=\mathrm{diag}(\gamma_1,\dots,\gamma_N)$ and $(x\odot x)_i=x_i^2$.
If the sextic term is neglected at leading order, then
\[
x^{(0)}_S = -\frac{1}{u}\,(B^{(S)})^{-1}r,
\]
and a mixed phase with all $x_i>0$ exists whenever $x^{(0)}_S \in \mathbb{R}^N_{>0}$.


\section{Multi-Shell Non-Inversion Theorem: A Sufficient Condition via Matrix Ordering}

Define the minimized free energy
\[
F_S^{\min}(r):=\min_{x\in\mathbb{R}^N_{\ge 0}} F_S(x).
\]

Assume:
(i) $\gamma_i\equiv \gamma$ is structure-independent (sextic neutrality),
(ii) the quartic matrices are positive definite for all candidate structures,
\[
B^{(S)}\succ 0,\qquad \forall S\in\mathcal{S},
\]
and (iii) there exists a reference structure $S_\star$ such that, for every $S\in\mathcal{S}$,
\[
B^{(S)} - B^{(S_\star)} \succeq 0
\quad\text{(Loewner ordering)}.
\]

Then for every $r$ in the fluctuation-dominated regime where quartic control applies,
\[
F_{S_\star}^{\min}(r)\le F_S^{\min}(r)\qquad \forall S\in\mathcal{S}.
\]
Hence $S_\star$ is the structurally selected phase and no inversion occurs throughout that regime.

\emph{Sketch of mechanism.}
For fixed $x\ge 0$, the quartic contribution satisfies
$x^{\mathsf T}B^{(S_\star)}x \le x^{\mathsf T}B^{(S)}x$ by the ordering assumption.
Taking minima over $x\ge 0$ preserves the inequality up to the sextic-neutral remainder.


\section{Multi-Shell Robust Selection Theorem in the Quartic-Only Limit}

Assume sextic neutrality and neglect sextic terms at leading order.
If the unconstrained minimizer is interior for each structure,
\[
x^{(0)}_S = -\frac{1}{u}\,(B^{(S)})^{-1}r \in \mathbb{R}^N_{>0},
\]
then the minimized free energy admits the closed form
\[
F_{S,\mathrm{quartic}}^{\min}(r)
=
-\frac{1}{2u}\, r^{\mathsf T}\,(B^{(S)})^{-1}r.
\]

Therefore structural selection reduces to the ordering of the quadratic form
$r^{\mathsf T}(B^{(S)})^{-1}r$:
\[
S_\star
=
\arg\min_{S\in\mathcal{S}} F_{S,\mathrm{quartic}}^{\min}(r)
=
\arg\max_{S\in\mathcal{S}} r^{\mathsf T}(B^{(S)})^{-1}r.
\]

A sufficient \emph{non-inversion} condition is:
\[
(B^{(S_\star)})^{-1} - (B^{(S)})^{-1} \succeq 0
\quad \forall S\in\mathcal{S},
\]
which implies
$r^{\mathsf T}(B^{(S_\star)})^{-1}r \ge r^{\mathsf T}(B^{(S)})^{-1}r$ for all $r$.


\section{Multi-Shell Strong Resonance Criterion (Simultaneous Near-Saturation)}

Define the correlation (Cauchy) ratios
\[
\kappa^{(S)}_{ij}
:=
\frac{\beta^{(S)}_{ij}}{\sqrt{\beta^{(S)}_{i}\beta^{(S)}_{j}}}
\qquad (i\neq j).
\]

If $|\kappa^{(S)}_{ij}|\to 1$ for multiple pairs $(i,j)$ simultaneously, the system approaches a strong resonance network.
A robust sufficient condition for avoiding ill-conditioning and preserving mixed-phase well-posedness is the uniform bound
\[
\max_{i\neq j} |\kappa^{(S)}_{ij}| \le 1-\delta
\quad \text{for some } \delta\in(0,1),
\]
which implies $B^{(S)}\succ 0$ with a condition number bounded in terms of $\delta$ and $\{\beta^{(S)}_i\}$.

Conversely, multi-shell strong resonance occurs when the smallest eigenvalue $\lambda_{\min}(B^{(S)})$ approaches $0^+$:
\[
\lambda_{\min}(B^{(S)}) \downarrow 0,
\]
in which case mixed-phase amplitudes may become large and higher-order terms (including loop-renormalized sextic and beyond)
must be retained for a controlled minimization.


\section{All-Loop Closure Statement (Structural Polynomiality in Multi-Shell Form)}

At loop order $\ell$, the fluctuation correction to the quartic sector can be absorbed into the renormalized matrix
\[
B^{(S)} \ \mapsto\ B^{(S)}_{\mathrm{eff}} = B^{(S)} + \Delta B^{(S)}_{(\ell)},
\]
where each entry $\Delta B^{(S)}_{(\ell),ij}$ depends on $S$ only through finitely many additive resonance invariants
built from discrete star data across shells.

If these invariants reduce to polynomials in a finite set of structural generators (e.g.\ $C_S$ and mixed-shell counts),
then each entry is a polynomial with nonnegative coefficients in those generators, and the multi-shell selection problem
remains closed at all loop orders by replacing $B^{(S)}$ with $B^{(S)}_{\mathrm{eff}}$ in the theorems above.

\section{Higher-Loop Routing Decomposition: Discrete External Stars vs. Continuous Internal Momenta}

Fix a candidate structure $S$ with star set(s) providing discrete external wavevectors.
For any $\ell$-loop connected $4$-point 1PI diagram $D$ ($\ell\ge 2$), assign the external legs to discrete star
vectors $p_a\in S$ $(a=1,2,3,4)$, while the loop lines carry continuous momenta $q_\alpha$ integrated over the
shell thickness.

The diagram amplitude can be written as
\[
\mathcal{A}_D(S)
=
\sum_{(p_1,\dots,p_4)\in S^4}
\int \prod_{\alpha=1}^{L} \frac{d^d q_\alpha}{(2\pi)^d}\;
\mathcal{I}_D\!\left(\{q_\alpha\};\{p_a\}\right),
\]
where all structure dependence enters only through the discrete assignment $\{p_a\}$ and the momentum-conservation
constraints at vertices.

Although higher-loop diagrams exhibit \emph{mode-sharing} (internal lines shared by multiple vertices), one may
eliminate internal routing variables along a spanning tree of $D$ to obtain a \emph{majorant} counting bound:
there exist constants $c_{D,m}\ge 0$ depending only on the topology of $D$ such that
\[
N_D(S)\;\le\;\sum_{m=2}^{\ell+2} c_{D,m}\, I_{2m}(S),
\]
where $N_D(S)$ counts admissible discrete resonance assignments compatible with $D$, and $I_{2m}(S)$ are additive
$2m$-wave resonance invariants built solely from the star geometry of $S$.

Consequently, the $\ell$-loop correction to the effective quartic sector can be expressed as a finite combination
of structural invariants with nonnegative coefficients:
\[
\Delta \beta^{(\ell)}_{\mathrm{eff}}(S)
=
u^{\ell+1}\sum_{m=2}^{\ell+2} a_{\ell m}\, I_{2m}(S),
\qquad a_{\ell m}\ge 0,
\]
so the all-loop structural dependence is closed by a finite invariant tower.


\section{Sextic Neutrality: Stabilization Without Affecting Structural Selection}

Consider the Landau functional for a given structure $S$ (single-shell or multi-shell reduced to amplitudes):
\[
f_S(A)
=
\frac{\alpha}{2}A^2+\frac{\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S}}{4}A^4+\frac{\gamma}{6}A^6,
\qquad \gamma>0.
\]

Assume \emph{sextic neutrality}: the stabilizing sextic coefficient $\gamma$ is structure-independent across the
candidate set (e.g.\ SC/FCC/BCC), or differs only by subleading corrections in the fluctuation-dominated regime.

Then structural selection is determined solely by the effective quartic coefficient:
for any $S_1,S_2$,
\[
\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S_1}<\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S_2}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\min_A f_{S_1}(A)\;<\;\min_A f_{S_2}(A).
\]
Therefore the sextic term stabilizes the free energy (allowing first-order transition) but does not alter the
ordering of structures, which is fixed by quartic fluctuation selection.

\section{Physical Interpretation of Fluctuation–Driven Structural Selection}

The mathematical results obtained above admit a simple physical interpretation.
Structural selection in the fluctuation–dominated Brazovskii regime is governed
by the geometry of resonance networks formed by wavevectors on the instability shell.

Assume the order parameter field satisfies the symmetry
\[
\Psi \rightarrow -\Psi .
\]

This symmetry forbids all odd invariants, in particular the cubic Landau term
\[
\int d^3x\,\Psi^3(x) = 0 .
\]

Consequently, the leading nonlinear interaction responsible for structural
selection is the quartic interaction

\[
F_4 \sim \Psi^4 .
\]

Therefore the ordering of candidate structures is determined entirely by
four–wave resonances among shell wavevectors.


\section{Resonance Geometry and Quartic Invariants}

Let the star set of a structure $S$ be denoted by
\[
S=\{k_i\}_{i=1}^{N_S}, \qquad |k_i|=q_0 .
\]

Quartic interactions arise from resonant combinations satisfying

\[
k_1+k_2+k_3+k_4=0 .
\]

The number of such resonant configurations defines the quartic structural
invariant

\[
\beta_S .
\]

Physically, $\beta_S$ measures the connectivity of the resonance network
formed by the shell wavevectors.


\section{Fluctuation Energy and Resonance Connectivity}

Fluctuations propagate along resonant wavevector combinations.
The larger the number of available resonant loops, the more efficiently
fluctuations can redistribute energy.

Therefore structures with higher resonance connectivity reduce the
fluctuation free energy.

At leading order the free energy takes the form

\[
F_S \sim \beta_S A^4 .
\]

Thus the structure with the smallest quartic invariant minimizes the
free energy.


\section{Why the BCC Structure is Selected}

Among the cubic candidate structures

\[
S \in \{\mathrm{SC},\mathrm{FCC},\mathrm{BCC}\},
\]

the body–centered cubic star possesses

\begin{itemize}
\item the largest number of shell vectors,
\item the largest number of closed four–wave loops,
\item the highest resonance connectivity.
\end{itemize}

Consequently

\[
\beta_{\mathrm{BCC}}
<
\beta_{\mathrm{FCC}}
<
\beta_{\mathrm{SC}} .
\]

The free–energy ordering therefore becomes

\[
F_{\mathrm{BCC}} < F_{\mathrm{FCC}} < F_{\mathrm{SC}} .
\]

This establishes the fluctuation–driven selection of the BCC structure.


\section{Multi–Shell Extension}

In the presence of multiple shells the quartic interaction becomes

\[
F_4
=
\sum_{i,j}
B_{ij} A_i^2 A_j^2 ,
\]

where the matrix $B_{ij}$ contains both self–shell and mixed–shell
quartic invariants.

Structural stability requires

\[
B \succ 0 .
\]

The minimized free energy in the quartic approximation is

\[
F_{\min}
=
-\frac{1}{2u} r^{T} B^{-1} r .
\]

Hence structural selection is controlled by the inverse quartic matrix.


\section{Absence of Structural Inversion}

Mixed–shell couplings satisfy the geometric bound

\[
\beta_{ij}^2 \le \beta_i \beta_j ,
\]

which implies

\[
\det B > 0 .
\]

Therefore the quartic matrix remains positive definite and the
ordering of structures cannot invert throughout the
fluctuation–dominated regime.


\section{Physical Principle}

The fluctuation–driven selection mechanism can therefore be summarized as

\[
\text{Structure Selection}
=
\text{Maximal Resonance Connectivity}.
\]

The body–centered cubic structure maximizes the connectivity of
four–wave resonance networks and therefore minimizes the fluctuation
free energy.

This explains the robustness of BCC selection in Brazovskii–type
systems across single–shell, multi–shell, and higher–loop regimes.

\newpage

\section{Main Theorem: Robust BCC Selection from Quartic Fluctuation Resonances}

We consider an isotropic Brazovskii-type system in $d=3$ with instability shell
\[
|q| = q_0 .
\]

Let $S$ denote a candidate cubic structure with star set
\[
S=\{k_i\}_{i=1}^{N_S}, \qquad |k_i|=q_0 .
\]

Assume the order parameter satisfies the symmetry
\[
\Psi \rightarrow -\Psi ,
\]
so that all odd invariants vanish and the leading nonlinear interaction is quartic.

The fluctuation–induced free energy takes the Landau form
\[
F_S =
rA^2
+
\frac{\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S}}{4}A^4
+
\frac{\gamma}{6}A^6 ,
\qquad \gamma>0 .
\]

The effective quartic coefficient is
\[
\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S}
=
\beta_{\mathrm{MF},S}
+
\sum_{\ell\ge1}
\Delta \beta^{(\ell)}_S ,
\]
where $\ell$ denotes loop order.

\subsection*{Structural Invariants}

Quartic interactions arise from four–wave resonance constraints
\[
k_1+k_2+k_3+k_4=0 .
\]

The number of such configurations defines a structural invariant
\[
C_S .
\]

At higher loop order the fluctuation corrections take the form
\[
\Delta \beta^{(\ell)}_S
=
u^{\ell+1}
\sum_{m=2}^{\ell+2}
a_{\ell m} I_{2m}(S),
\qquad a_{\ell m}\ge0 ,
\]
where $I_{2m}(S)$ are resonance invariants determined solely by the
geometry of the star $S$.

Consequently the structural dependence of the quartic sector can be
written as a polynomial
\[
\beta_{\mathrm{eff},S}
=
P(C_S),
\]
with non–negative coefficients.

\subsection*{Selection Principle}

Because the coefficients of $P$ are non–negative, the ordering induced
by $C_S$ is preserved to all loop orders.

For cubic stars in $d=3$ one finds
\[
C_{\mathrm{BCC}}
>
C_{\mathrm{FCC}}
>
C_{\mathrm{SC}} .
\]

Therefore
\[
\beta_{\mathrm{eff,BCC}}
<
\beta_{\mathrm{eff,FCC}}
<
\beta_{\mathrm{eff,SC}} .
\]

Since the sextic term is structure–neutral, the minimized free energies
satisfy

\[
F_{\mathrm{BCC}}
<
F_{\mathrm{FCC}}
<
F_{\mathrm{SC}} .
\]

\subsection*{Universality}

The result extends to the most general isotropic quartic vertex
\[
F_4
=
\int
F(\alpha)
\Psi_{k_1}\Psi_{k_2}\Psi_{k_3}\Psi_{k_4},
\]
provided
\[
F(\alpha)\ge0 .
\]

In this case the structural invariant becomes a weighted resonance
count $\Lambda_S$, but the hierarchy
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}
>
\Lambda_{\mathrm{FCC}}
>
\Lambda_{\mathrm{SC}}
\]
remains unchanged.

\subsection*{Conclusion}

Under the assumptions of

\begin{itemize}
\item $\mathbb{Z}_2$ symmetry (no cubic invariants),
\item isotropy of the instability shell,
\item positive quartic interaction kernel,
\item fluctuation–dominated Brazovskii regime,
\end{itemize}

the body–centered cubic structure maximizes resonance connectivity of
four–wave interactions and therefore minimizes the fluctuation free
energy.

Hence BCC selection is robust across single–shell, multi–shell, and
all–loop regimes of quartic fluctuation dynamics.
